Mudarib's Expenses in His Own City: From His Wealth
Ruling with expansion on mudarib's expenses in his city

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mudarib's living expenses and identify the conditions under which he may take expenses from the mudarabah capital.

Core Idea
A mudarib's expenses are from his own wealth in his own city; only travel and trade-related stays shift them to the capital.

This lesson examines the precise Hanafi ruling on the mudarib's living expenses (nafaqah). While the mudarib is entitled to take necessary expenses from the mudarabah capital when traveling for trade, his personal expenses in the city where he resides—whether his original homeland or a temporary home—must come from his own pocket. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the key distinctions: a valid mudarabah, the meaning of 'his city,' and the effect of marriage and returning from travel. We will walk through these conditions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Ruling and Its Scope
Recognize that the matn states expenses in the mudarib's city are from his own wealth. The commentary clarifies that this applies only to valid mudarabah; in an invalid mudarabah, the mudarib is an ajir and does not get expenses from the capital.
The base ruling is straightforward, but the scope is critical.
2
Define 'His City'
The commentary expands 'his city' to mean any place of residence, whether original homeland or not. The mudarib's stay there determines the source of expenses.
Even a borrowed home counts if the mudarib is not there for trade.
3
Distinguish Travel and Return
If the mudarib travels for trade and returns to the city, his expenses become chargeable to the mudarabah capital because his return is for business. The rule changes based on purpose of stay.
This exception is a key point often misunderstood.
4
Assess the Effect of Marriage
Marriage and settling down in a city transform a temporary residence into a permanent home, ending the right to capital expenses. The mudarib is considered to be in his own city.
This prevents abuse of the travel exception.
5
Evaluate the Critique of al-Durr al-Mukhtar
The commentator refines the ruling: taking other wealth does not automatically cut off expenses. The correct condition is whether the stay is for the mudarib's own purposes or for mudarabah.
This critique highlights the importance of the underlying rationale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل فيما يفعله المضارب ونفقة مضارب عمل في مصره في ماله كدوائه [1] قوله : ونفقة مضارب؛ أي مضاربة صحيحة، فإن المضارب في المضاربة الفاسدة لا يستحق النفقة؛ لكونه أجيرا، كما صرحوا به. [2] قوله: في مصره؛ قال البرجندي في شرح النقاية: فالمراد بمصره المصر الذي أقام فيه سواء كان وطناً أصلياً أو لا، فلو وقع عقد المضاربة بالكوفة، وهي ليست وطناً أصلياً له فما دام فيها نفقته في ماله، فإن سافر وعاد إلى الكوفة في تجارته كان نفقته في مال المضاربة. كذا في الخزانة. انتهى. وقال في المبسوط: ولو دفع المال إليه مضاربة، وهما بالكوفة وليست الكوفة بوطن للمضارب لم ينفق على نفسه من المال ما دام في الكوفة؛ لأن إقامته فيها ليست للمضاربة، فلا يستوجب النفقة ما لم يخرج منها إلى وطنه، ثم عاد إليها في تجارته، أنفق في الكوفة من مال المضاربة؛ لأنه وطنه بها كان مستعاراً وقد انتقض بالسفر. فرجوعه بعد ذلك إلى الكوفة وذهابه إلى مصر آخر سواء، وإن تزوج امرأة واتخذها وطناً زالت نفقته من مال المضاربة؛ لأن مقامه لها بعدما تزوج كان لأجل أهله بمنزلة وطنه الأصلي، ذكره العلامة الطحطاوي نقلاً عن ملكي، وهكذا في البحر نقلا عن البدائع والمحيط والفتاوى الظهيرية. فما في الدر المختار: إذا نوى الإقامة بمصر ولم يتخذه داراً فله النفقة ما لم يأخذ مالاً؛ لأنه لم يحتبس بمالها. انتهى. لا يخفى ما فيه من الإيجار المخل، فإنه يفيد أنه إذا أخذ مالاً غير مال المضاربة، وأقام بالكوفة لا نفقة له وليس كذلك، فالمقصود ما ذكرناه نقلاً عن المبسوط وغيره. [3] قوله: في ماله؛ لأنه لم يحتبس نفسه لأجل المضاربة، بل هو ساكن بالمسكن الأصلي. [4] قوله: كدوائه؛ فإنه في ماله مطلقاً، سواء كان في الحضر أو في السفر.
Chapter on what the mudarib does. And the expense of a mudarib who works in his own city is from his own wealth, like his medicine. [1] He said: 'And the expense of a mudarib' means a valid mudarabah, for the mudarib in an invalid mudarabah does not deserve expenses because he is a hired worker, as they stated. [2] He said: 'in his city.' Al-Birjandi said in Sharh al-Nuqayah: What is meant by 'his city' is the city where he resides, whether it is his original homeland or not. So if the mudarabah contract is made in Kufa and it is not his original homeland, as long as he is there his expenses are from his own wealth. But if he travels and returns to Kufa for his trade, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital. This is in al-Khazanah. End. And in al-Mabsut it is said: If someone gives him capital for mudarabah while they are in Kufa and Kufa is not the mudarib's homeland, he does not spend from the capital on himself as long as he is in Kufa, because his stay there is not for mudarabah, so he does not become entitled to expenses unless he leaves from there to his homeland and then returns to it in his trade. Then he may spend in Kufa from the mudarabah capital because his dwelling there was borrowed and was terminated by travel. So his return after that to Kufa and his going to another city are the same. And if he marries a woman and takes her as a home, his expenses from the mudarabah capital cease because his stay after marriage is for his family, like his original homeland. This was mentioned by Allama al-Tahtawi quoting from Malki, and likewise in al-Bahr citing al-Bada'i', al-Muhit, and al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyyah. What is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: if he intends to reside in a city and does not take it as a permanent home, he gets expenses as long as he does not take wealth; because he did not detain himself for its wealth. End. The hidden defect in this is clear, for it indicates that if he takes wealth other than mudarabah capital and stays in Kufa, he has no expenses, but that is not so. What is intended is what we mentioned citing al-Mabsut and others. [3] He said: 'from his own wealth' because he did not detain himself for the sake of mudarabah, but rather he is residing in his original home. [4] He said: 'like his medicine' for it is from his own wealth absolutely, whether he is in residence or on a journey.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the mudarib's nafaqah (living expenses). The core ruling is that when a mudarib is working in his own city, his personal expenses and even his medicine come from his own wealth, not from the mudarabah capital. The commentary clarifies important distinctions: The mudarabah must be valid; in a fasid (invalid) mudarabah, the mudarib is considered an ajir (hired worker) and does not get nafaqah from the capital. The term 'his city' refers to any city where he resides, even if it is not his original homeland. If the mudarabah contract is made in a city where the mudarib is a temporary resident, his expenses are from his own wealth as long as he stays there without traveling for trade. But if he travels for trade and returns to that city, then his expenses can be taken from the mudarabah capital because his return is for the trade. Once he marries and settles down, he loses the right to take nafaqah from the capital because his residence becomes permanent for family reasons, not for mudarabah. The passage also critiques a ruling in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, affirming that the correct view is from al-Mabsut: taking other wealth does not automatically cut off nafaqah.

Key Terms
نفقة (nafaqah): Living expenses including food, clothing, and medicine necessary for daily life.
مصر (miṣr): A city or town where the mudarib resides, whether originally or temporarily.
أجير (ajīr): A hired worker who receives a fixed wage; in invalid mudarabah, the mudarib is treated as an ajir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib enters a mudarabah contract in a city that is not his original homeland and stays there without traveling for trade, where do his living expenses come from?
A) From the mudarabah capital because he is working.
B) From his own wealth because his stay is not for mudarabah.
C) He has no expenses; he must live without spending.
D) The investor must provide his expenses.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's living expenses come from his own wealth when he is in his own city; only when his stay is directly for mudarabah trade does he draw from the capital.

Definition and Ruling of Deposits (Wadi'ah)
Linguistic definition and legal ruling of wadi'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic definition of wadi'ah, identify the parties involved, and explain why preserving deposits is fard (obligatory) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Deposits (al-wadi'ah) are pure trusts; safeguarding and returning them is an individual obligation (fard) on the depository.

This lesson introduces the Book of Deposits. You will learn the linguistic roots of wadi'ah, the roles of depositor and depository, and the legal ruling—why preserving deposits is not just recommended but obligatory, with evidence from the hadith. The passage also clarifies that wadi'ah is a trust (amanah) and not a guarantee, unless negligence occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic meaning
Examine the etymology: wadi'ah (فَعِيلَة) means 'deposited thing', derived from 'ease' (الدعة) or 'absolute leaving' (الودع). Note the dominant usage: handing over property for safekeeping.
The root appears in Quran (93:3) and hadith about abandoning congregations.
2
Distinguish the parties
Learn the roles: المودع (depositor) and المستودع (depository). The passage provides vowel marks and confirms that both parties can be described using the same root.
In the hadith 'الْمُودَعُ' refers to the thing deposited.
3
Recognize the legal ruling (fard)
The text states that preserving all trusts (including wadi'ah) is 'فرض' (fard). Understand that this is an individual obligation on the depository to safeguard and return the deposit.
The ruling is derived from the general command to fulfill trusts and the specific hadith.
4
Locate the dalil (proof)
The author cites the hadith: 'على اليد ما أخذت حتى تؤديه' (The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it). Identify its source: Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, graded hasan.
This hadith is a strong proof (zanni al-thubut) but supported by consensus.
5
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof establishes the ruling)
The phrase 'حتى تؤديه' (until it returns it) indicates an obligation (wujub) to return the deposit. Liability remains with the depository until return, reinforcing the duty of preservation.
Hanafi usul: al-amr yufidu al-wujub (a command indicates obligation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة
[١] قوله : كتاب الوديعة ؛ لا خفاء في اشتراكه مع ما قبله في الحكم، وهو الأمانة. والوديعة : فعيلة، يعني مفعولة، وأودعت زيداً مالاً : دفعته إليه ليكون عنده وديعة ، وجمعها ودائع، واشتقاقها من الدعة وهي الراحة، أو أخذته منه وديعة، فيكون الفعلُ من الأضداد، ولكن الفعل في الدفع أشهر، واستودعته مالاً : دفعته له وديعة يحفظه. كذا في «المصباح»). وقال في «المغرب»(۲) : أودعتُ زيداً مالاً ، واستودعته إياه : إذا دفعته إليه ليكون عنده، فأنا مودع ومستودع بالكسر ، وزيـد مـودَع ومستودع بالفتح ، والمـال مـودع ومستودع ؛ أي وديعة. انتهى. وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز (۳) : وفي اللغة : الوديعة مشتقة من الودع ، وهـو مطلق الترك ، قال له لا له : «الينتهين أقوام عن ودعتهم الجماعات ، أو ليختمن على قلوبهم ثم ليكتبن من الغافلين ) : أي [على] تركهم إيَّاها ، قال الله عَ : مَا وَدَعَكَ رَبُّكَ وَمَا عَلَى ا قُرئت بالتخفيف والتشديد. والمودع الشيء المتروك ، قال : «الإيمان نور الله تعالى أودعه في قلوب المؤمنين»، فعلى العبدِ أن يسأل التوفيق على حفظ وديعة الله تعالى، وعلى حفظ جميع الأمانات، فإنّه فرض. قال النبي : «على اليد ما أخذت حتى تؤدّيه (٦)، رواه أبو داود والترمذي،
وقال : حديث حسن
The Book of Deposits. [1] His statement: 'Book of Deposits'; there is no hiddenness in its sharing with what preceded it in ruling, and it is the trust. And 'al-wadi'ah' is a pattern فَعيلَة meaning مَفْعُولَة. 'I deposited money with Zayd' means I handed it over to him to be a deposit with him. Its plural is ودائع. Its derivation is from الدعة meaning ease, or 'I took it from him as a deposit', thus the verb becomes from antonyms, but the verb in the sense of handing over is more famous. 'I entrusted money to him' means I handed it over to him as a deposit for him to keep. Thus in 'al-Misbah'. And he said in 'al-Mughrib': 'I deposited money with Zayd and I entrusted it to him' means I handed it over to him to be with him, so I am مُودِع and مُسْتَوْدِع (with kasra), and Zayd is مُودَع and مُسْتَوْدَع (with fatha), and the money is مُودَع and مُسْتَوْدَع, i.e. a deposit. End. And al-Zayla'i said in 'Sharh al-Kanz': In language, 'al-wadi'ah' is derived from الودع which is absolute leaving. He (the Prophet) said: 'People will certainly desist from their abandoning the congregations, or Allah will certainly seal their hearts, then they will surely be written among the heedless.' i.e., [due to] their abandoning of them. Allah Almighty said: 'Your Lord has not forsaken you nor does He hate you' (ad-Duha:3), recited with tashdid and takhfif. And 'al-muwda'' is the thing left. He (the Prophet) said: 'Faith is the light of Allah Almighty which He has deposited in the hearts of the believers.' So upon the servant is to ask for success in preserving the deposit of Allah Almighty, and in preserving all trusts, for it is an obligation. The Prophet said: 'The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it,' narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, who said: It is a hasan hadith.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a combination of linguistic definition and legal ruling on deposits (wadi'ah). The author begins by introducing the Book of Deposits, noting its shared characteristic with previous topics, namely being a trust (amanah). He then provides a detailed linguistic analysis: wadi'ah is a verbal noun meaning 'deposited thing', derived either from 'ease' or from 'leaving', with the dominant usage being the act of handing over property for safekeeping. The terms mudi' (depositor) and mustawdi' (depository) are clarified with vowel markings. Al-Zayla'i adds that wadi'ah literally means 'abandoned thing', citing a hadith warning against abandoning congregational prayer and a Quranic verse (93:3) using the same root. The passage then transitions to the legal ruling: preserving all trusts, including wadi'ah, is fard (obligatory). The author supports this with the hadith: 'The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it' (hasan, narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi). This hadith establishes the depository's liability for the deposit until it is returned. The overall message is that deposits are sacred trusts that must be safeguarded, and neglecting them incurs sin.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): Property handed over to someone for safekeeping; a trust.
مودع (mūdiʿ): The person who deposits property (depositor).
مستودع (mustawdaʿ): The person who receives the deposit for safekeeping (depository).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal ruling (hukm) regarding the preservation of deposits (wadi'ah) according to the passage?
A) Sunnah mu'akkadah
B) Mubah
C) Fard (obligatory)
D) Makruh

Key Takeaway
Deposits are sacred trusts; their preservation is an individual obligation on the depository, supported by the hadith 'The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it.'

Definition, Rukn, and Conditions of Wadi'ah
Opens the Book of Deposits with definitions and rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah linguistically and legally, identify its rukn and conditions, and apply the hadith proof to explain why it is an amanah.

Core Idea
Wadi'ah is an amanah — the depositary is a trustee, not a guarantor, unless negligent.

This passage opens the Book of Wadi'ah by grounding the legal concept in language and revelation. We will dissect the definition of wadi'ah, its essential pillar (rukn), and the conditions that must be met for a valid deposit. Understanding these fundamentals is critical because the entire law of deposits turns on the principle that wadi'ah is a trust, not a guarantee.

Guided Path
1
Examine the linguistic roots of 'wadi'ah'
The passage derives wadi'ah from al-wada' (leaving) and al-da'ah (comfort). Explore how the word shapes the legal concept.
2
Identify the legal definition and its implications
The commentator states wadi'ah is an amanah in Shari'ah. Understand what amanah means for liability.
Amanah = trust; depositary not liable unless negligence.
3
Pinpoint the rukn and its possible forms
The rukn is the offer and acceptance, both of which can be explicit or implicit (kinayah or indication).
Silence when item is placed counts as acceptance by indication.
4
List the conditions for validity
Property must be tangible and capable of being taken into hand; depositor must be mukallaf for the obligation to preserve.
A bird in the air or sunken wealth cannot be deposited.
5
Locate the dalil and explain its wajh al-dalalah
The hadith 'ala al-yad ma akhadhat...' proves the duty to return, establishing wadi'ah as amanah.
The hadith is zanni al-thubut but widely accepted.

وروي أنّ زليخا لما ابتليت بالفقر وابيضت عيناها من الحزن على يوسف جلست على قارعة الطريق في زي الفقراء، فقامت تنادي : أيها الملك ؛ اسمع كلامي، فوقف يوسف ، فقالت : الأمانة أقامت المملوك مقامَ الملوك، والخيانة أقامت الملوك مقام المملوك، فسأل عنها، فقيل : إنّها زليخاً، فتزوجها مرحمة عليها. انتهى بأدنى اختصار.

Book of Wadi'ah. [1] His saying: Book of Wadi'ah; there is no concealment in its sharing with what preceded it in the ruling, which is trust (amanah). And wadi'ah: fa'ilah, meaning maf'ulah. 'I entrusted Zaid with wealth' means I gave it to him to be with him as a deposit; its plural is wada'i'; its derivation is from al-da'ah which is comfort, or 'I took it from him as a deposit', so the verb is from opposites, but the verb in giving is more famous. 'I entrusted him with wealth' means I gave it to him as a deposit to preserve. Thus in al-Misbah. And he said in al-Mughrib: 'I entrusted Zaid with wealth, and I made him a depositary of it' means I gave it to him to be with him; so I am a depositor (mudi') and a depositary (mustawdi') with kasra, and Zaid is deposited (muda') and entrusted (mustawda') with fatha, and the wealth is deposited (muda') and entrusted (mustawda'), i.e. wadi'ah. End. And al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: In language, wadi'ah is derived from al-wada', which is absolute leaving. The Prophet said: 'Let people desist from leaving the congregations, or Allah will seal their hearts, then they will be written among the heedless' meaning [upon] their abandoning them. Allah said: 'Your Lord has not forsaken you (wada'aka) nor does He hate you' recited with takhfif and tashdid. And al-muda' is the thing left. He said: 'Faith is the light of Allah the Exalted which He deposits (awda'ahu) in the hearts of the believers.' So upon the servant is to ask for success in preserving the deposit of Allah and in preserving all trusts, for it is an obligation. The Prophet said: 'The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it.' Narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Tirmidhi, and he said: hasan hadith. And he said: 'Indeed Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners.' Preserving them brings happiness in both abodes, and betrayal brings misery in them. He said: 'Trust leads to wealth, and betrayal leads to poverty.' And it is narrated that Zulaykha, when she was afflicted with poverty and her eyes became white from grief over Yusuf, sat at the roadside in the garb of the poor. She stood up calling: 'O king, listen to my words.' Yusuf stopped, and she said: 'Trust raised the slave to the position of kings, and betrayal raised kings to the position of slaves.' He asked about her, and it was said: She is Zulaykha. So he married her out of mercy on her. End with slight abbreviation. And in the Shari'ah, what the commentator mentioned by his saying: 'It is a trust... etc.' Its rukn: the offer explicitly, as his saying: 'I entrust this garment to you' for example, or implicitly, as when a man says: 'Give me a thousand dirhams', or he says to a man in whose hand is a garment: 'Give me', and he says: 'I give it to you', this is as a deposit (wadi'), as stated in al-Muhit. Because giving (i'ta') can mean gift (hibah) or deposit (wadi'ah), and wadi'ah is the lesser (adna) and it is certain, so it becomes a metaphor (kinayah). Or an action, as if he places his garment in front of a man and says nothing, it is a deposit (ida'). And acceptance by the depositary (mudi') explicitly, like his saying: 'I accept it' and the like, or by indication, as if he remains silent when it is placed before him, for it is acceptance by indication. So if he says: 'I do not accept', he is not a depositary, because the indication did not exist. Its condition: that the wealth be capable of being taken into hand (qabilan li-ithbat al-yad 'alayhi), so if one deposits a runaway slave, a bird in the air, or wealth fallen in the sea, it is not valid. And that the depositor (mudi') be accountable (mukallaf) is a condition for the obligation of preservation upon him, so if one deposits with a boy and he consumes it, he does not guarantee; but if it was a restricted slave (abdun mahjurun), he guarantees after manumission. Thus in al-Muhit and others. Its cause (sabab): the attachment of possible preservation (al-baqa' al-maqdur) through the mutual transaction (al-ta'ati). The legality (shar'iyyah) of deposit is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Wadi'ah (Deposits) from the sharh. The commentator begins by noting that the ruling of wadi'ah shares with the previous topic (presumably regarding trusts) the same basic ruling: it is an amanah (trust), not a guarantee. He then provides an extensive linguistic analysis: wadi'ah is the object of the verb (maf'ulah), derived from al-da'ah (comfort) or al-wada' (leaving). The verb 'awda'tu' can mean either 'I gave as a deposit' or 'I took as a deposit', though the former is more common. The author cites al-Misbah, al-Mughrib, and al-Zayla'i to show the linguistic range. He then recounts a hadith warning against abandoning congregations and the verse 'Your Lord has not forsaken you' (al-Duha: 3) to illustrate the root meaning of 'leaving'. He emphasizes that faith is a deposit from Allah, and the servant must guard all trusts as a fard. The hadith 'The hand is responsible for what it has taken until it returns it' (narrated by Abu Dawud and Tirmidhi) and the verse 'Indeed Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (al-Nisa': 58) form the primary dalil. The story of Zulaykha and Yusuf illustrates the consequence of trust vs. betrayal. Then the commentator gives the legal definition: wadi'ah is an amanah. The rukn of wadi'ah is the offer (ijab) from the depositor, which can be explicit (e.g., 'I entrust this garment to you') or implicit (e.g., 'Give me a thousand dirhams' where context indicates deposit). Acceptance (qabul) from the depositary can also be explicit or by indication (e.g., silence when the item is placed before him). If the depositary says 'I do not accept', there is no deposit. Conditions: the property must be tangible and capable of being taken into hand (so depositing a runaway slave, bird in air, or sunken wealth is invalid); and the depositor must be mukallaf (responsible) for the depositary to be obligated to preserve it. If a deposit is made with a child or an insane person, and the property is destroyed, the depositary is not liable. For a restricted slave who is then freed, liability attaches after freedom. The cause (sabab) of the contract is the giving and taking that implies an intention to preserve. The legal validity of deposit is established by the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A specific type of trust (amanah) where one person gives property to another to keep and preserve without compensation.
الإيجاب (al-ijab): The offer made by the depositor indicating the transfer of property as a deposit.
القبول (al-qabul): The acceptance by the depositary, which can be explicit ('I accept') or by action/indication (silence).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the rukn (pillar) of a valid wadi'ah contract according to this passage?
A) The depositor must hand over the property physically.
B) The depositary must explicitly state 'I accept'.
C) There must be an offer (ijab) and acceptance (qabul), each of which can be explicit or implicit.
D) A written contract is required.

Key Takeaway
The rukn of wadi'ah is the offer and acceptance, which can be explicit or implicit; the property must be tangible and the depositor responsible; and the entire contract is built on the principle of trust (amanah).

Definition and Ruling of Wadi'ah (Deposit) as a Non-Liability Trust
Defines wadi'ah and explains its three components

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah, distinguish its three definitional components, state the Hanafi ruling that the depositary is not liable without transgression, and identify the pillar, condition, and cause of a valid deposit.

Core Idea
Wadi'ah is a trust for safekeeping; the depositary is not liable for loss unless he transgresses.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definition of wadi'ah (deposit) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. You will learn the precise legal meaning of 'trust left for safekeeping' and the critical distinction that the depositary is not a guarantor. We will also examine the proof texts from the Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma, and clarify the pillar (rukn), condition (shart), and cause (sabab) that make a deposit valid.

Guided Path
1
Establish the legal basis
Identify the three proofs (Quran, Sunnah, Ijma) cited for the permissibility and recommendation of deposit.
Note the Quranic verse 4:58 and the hadith on mutual assistance.
2
Break down the definition
Analyze the three components: it is a trust, left for safekeeping, and the depositary is not liable.
The term 'amanah' is a verbal noun used metaphorically for the object.
3
Distinguish from similar contracts
Consider the objection regarding ariyah (loan) and how the restriction 'only for safekeeping' differentiates it.
The lam in 'lil-hifdh' indicates sole purpose.
4
Understand the non-liability rule
Grasp that the depositary is only liable for transgression (ta'addi), not for mere loss.
The hadith from Ibn Majah, though weak, is supported by general principles.
5
Identify the validity requirements
List the rukn (offer), shart (graspable property), and sabab (mutual transaction).
Offer can be explicit or implicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرعية الإيداع ثابت بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع:
أما الكتاب؛ فقوله: ﴿إِنَّ اللَّهَ يَأْمُرُكُمْ أَنْ تُؤَدُّوا الْأَمَانَاتِ إِلَى أَهْلِهَا﴾ [النساء:58].
وأما السنة؛ فإنه ﷺ كان يودع ويستودع.
وأما الإجماع؛ فقد أجمعوا على أن قبول الوديعة من باب الإعانة، وهي مندوبة لقوله: ﴿وَتَعَاوَنُوا عَلَى الْبِرِّ وَالتَّقْوَىٰ﴾ [المائدة:2]، ولقوله: «والله في عون العبد ما دام العبد في عون أخيه».
[١] قوله: هي أمانة؛ أمن بالكسر أمانة، فهو أمين، ثم استعمل المصدر في الأعيان مجازاً، فقيل: الوديعة أمانة ونحوه، والجمع أمانات. فالأمانة مصدر، ثم سمى ما يأتمن عليه صاحبه أمانة تسمية بالمصدر.
[٢] قوله: تركت للحفظ؛ قيل لا بد في التعريف من قيد آخر، وهو قولنا: فقط؛ لأن العارية أمانة تركت للحفظ والانتفاع معاً. قلنا: اللام في قوله: للحفظ؛ للتعليل، فالمعنى أن علة تركها لا تكون إلا للحفظ.
[٣] قوله: فلا يضمنها المودع.. الخ؛ يعني فلا يضمن المودع الوديعة إذا هلكت من غير تعد منه، سواء أمكن التحرز عنه أو لا، وسواء هلك معها للمودع شيء أو لا. ووجه الضمان ما روى ابن ماجة عن عمرو بن شعيب عن أبيه عن جده: أن النبي ﷺ قال: «من أودع وديعة فلا ضمان عليه». [رواه ابن ماجة بإسناد ضعيف].
وركنها: الإيجاب صريحاً؛ لقوله: أودعتك هذا الثوب مثلاً، أو كناية؛ كما قال الرجل: أعطني ألف درهم، أو قال لرجل في يده ثوب: أعطني، فقال: أعطيتك، فهذا على الوديع، نص عليه في «المحيط».
وشرطها: كون المال قابلاً لإثبات اليد عليه، حتى لو أودع الآبق والطير الذي في الهواء، والمال الساقط في البحر لا يصح.
وسببها: تعلق البقاء المقدور بالتعاطي.
The legality of deposit is established by the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. As for the Book: His saying: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (4:58). As for the Sunnah: The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to deposit and be deposited. As for consensus: They have agreed that accepting a deposit is a form of assistance, and it is recommended due to His saying: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety' (5:2) and his saying: 'Allah is in the help of a servant as long as the servant is in the help of his brother.' [1] His saying: 'It is a trust': the root 'amana' (with kasra) gives 'amanah', so it is 'amin' (trustworthy). Then the verbal noun is used metaphorically for physical objects, so it is said: the deposit is a trust, and its plural is 'amanat'. So 'amanah' is a verbal noun, then what a person entrusts to another is called 'amanah' by naming with the verbal noun. [2] His saying: 'Left for safekeeping': It is said that the definition requires another restriction, which is our saying: 'only' because a loan (ariyah) is a trust left for both safekeeping and use. We say: the lam in 'for safekeeping' indicates purpose, so the meaning is that the reason for leaving it is only for safekeeping. [3] His saying: 'So the depositary is not liable...': meaning the depositary is not liable for the deposit if it perishes without his transgression, whether it was possible to guard against it or not, and whether something of the depositary perished with it or not. The basis of non-liability is what Ibn Majah narrated from 'Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather: The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever is given a deposit is under no liability.' [Ibn Majah, with a weak chain]. Its pillar (rukn): explicit offer, like saying 'I deposited this garment with you', or by implication, as when a man says 'Give me a thousand dirhams' or says to a man with a garment 'Give me' and he says 'I give it to you' – this is considered a deposit, as stated in al-Muhit. Its condition: the property must be capable of being taken into possession, so if one deposits a runaway slave, a bird in the air, or property fallen into the sea, it is not valid. Its cause (sabab): the attachment of possible permanence through mutual giving.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the definition of wadi'ah (deposit). The author expands upon the concise statement: 'It is a trust left for safekeeping; the depositary is not liable if it perishes.' The passage is of type definition_ruling with expansion_probe. The author first establishes the legitimacy of deposit through three proofs: the Quranic command to render trusts (4:58), the Prophet's practice of depositing and accepting deposits, and the consensus that accepting a deposit is meritorious, citing the verse on cooperation and the hadith on divine assistance. Then he explains each component of the definition. First, 'it is a trust' (amanah): linguistically, amanah is derived from amn, meaning security. It is a verbal noun used metaphorically for the object entrusted. Second, 'left for safekeeping': a potential objection is that the definition applies also to ariyah (loan), which is also a trust but for both safekeeping and use. The author responds that the lam in 'lil-hifdh' indicates purpose, so the sole reason for leaving the item is safekeeping, distinguishing it from loan. Third, 'so the depositary is not liable': the depositary (mudi') is not responsible for loss unless he transgresses. This holds whether the loss could have been prevented or not, and even if the depositary's own property perishes simultaneously. The proof is a hadith from Ibn Majah, though its chain is weak, it is supported by general principles. The passage also lists the rukn (pillar) as explicit or implicit offer, and the condition that the property must be physically graspable. The cause (sabab) is the mutual transaction implying permanence. This commentary clarifies that the Hanafi view is that the deposit is a non-liability trust, and the depositary's obligation is to safeguard, not to guarantee.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): A property left by one person with another for safekeeping.
أمانة (amanah): A trust; in this context, the object entrusted (deposit) is called amanah metaphorically.
مودَع (mudi'): The person with whom the deposit is left (depositary).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the depositary (mudi') liable for loss of a deposit according to the Hanafi school?
A) Always, regardless of circumstances
B) Only if he transgresses (commits ta'addi)
C) Only if the loss is due to his negligence
D) Only if he fails to return it on demand

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on wadi'ah is that it is a non-liability trust; the depositary is only responsible if he transgresses, not for mere loss.

Definition and Basic Ruling of Deposit (Wadi'ah)
Defining wadi'ah as a trust with no liability for accidental loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define wadi'ah as a trust left for safekeeping and state the Hanafi ruling that the depositary is not liable for loss unless he transgresses.

Core Idea
A deposit is a trust left for safekeeping; the depositary is not liable for loss unless he transgresses.

This lesson introduces the contract of wadi'ah (deposit) as defined in the Hanafi school. The passage establishes that a deposit is an amanah (trust) and the depositary is not liable if the item perishes without his transgression (ta'addi). We will examine the definition, its distinction from a generic amanah, and the textual proofs from Quran, Sunnah, and Ijma' that support this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being defined (wadi'ah: a property entrusted for safekeeping) and the central ruling: the depositary is not liable for loss unless he transgresses.
The ruling applies to both the depositor (who must return the item) and the depositary (who is not liable for accidental loss).
2
Locate the dalil
Find the proofs cited: Quran 4:58 (command to return trusts), the Prophet's practice of depositing and accepting deposits, and Ijma' that accepting a deposit is a recommended act of assistance.
The Quranic verse is the primary dalil; the hadith from Ibn Majah is noted as weak but supportive.
3
Analyze the definition
Break down the definition: wadi'ah is an amanah (trust) left specifically for safekeeping (li al-hifz). Distinguish it from 'ariyah (loan) which allows use, and from a non-contractual amanah (e.g., wind blowing an item into one's house).
The commentary emphasizes that the word 'amanah' is originally a verbal noun used metaphorically for the deposited object.
4
Apply the condition for liability
Identify the condition that triggers liability: transgression (ta'addi) by the depositary. Without transgression, the depositary is not liable even if the item is stolen or destroyed.
Transgression includes misuse, negligence, or refusing to return the item when demanded.

Book of Deposit: Its definition and rulings. (It is a trust left for safekeeping, so the depositary is not liable if it perishes:) i.e., without transgression from him. As for the Book: His statement: 'Indeed, Allah commands you to render trusts to their owners' (4:58). As for the Sunnah: He (ﷺ) used to deposit and accept deposits. As for consensus: they agreed that accepting a deposit is a form of assistance, and it is recommended due to His statement: 'And cooperate in righteousness and piety' (5:2), and his statement: 'Allah is in the aid of His servant as long as the servant is in the aid of his brother' (Muslim). [1] His statement: 'It is a trust'...
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Deposits (Kitab al-Wadi'ah) by defining the wadi'ah contract and establishing its core ruling. The matn declares that a deposit (wadi'ah) is an amanah (trust) left for safekeeping; the depositary (al-mudi') is not liable for its loss unless he commits transgression (ta'di). The commentator then provides three proofs: from the Quran (4:58) commanding the return of trusts, from the Sunnah (the Prophet's practice of depositing and accepting deposits), and from Ijma' (consensus that accepting deposits is a commendable act of cooperation). The commentary clarifies that the word 'amanah' is originally a verbal noun, used metaphorically for the deposited object. It distinguishes wadi'ah from 'ariyah (loan) by noting that wadi'ah is left solely for safekeeping, not for use. The ruling of non-liability is supported by a hadith from Ibn Majah, though its chain is weak; the commentator notes that the absence of liability is based on multiple evidences including the Quranic verse and the principle that the depositary is a volunteer (mutabarri') which does not impose liability. The passage also contrasts wadi'ah with a mere amanah (e.g., wind blowing a garment into one's house): in wadi'ah, the depositary is freed from liability upon returning the deposit by mutual agreement, whereas in a non-contractual amanah, liability may persist after returning if there was prior disagreement.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): A property entrusted to another for safekeeping without compensation.
الأمانة (al-amanah): A trust; here used both abstractly and metaphorically for the deposited item.
المودَع (al-mudi'): The depositary (the person with whom the deposit is left).
التعدي (al-ta'addi): Transgression; acting beyond the scope of permission, such as using the deposit or being negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The hadith 'From whom a deposit is taken, there is no liability'
C) Consensus (Ijma') that accepting deposits is mandatory
D) The principle that the depositary is a guarantor

Key Takeaway
A deposit (wadi'ah) is a trust left for safekeeping; the depositary is not liable for loss unless he commits transgression (ta'addi).

Depositary's Right to Keep Deposit with His Household
Ruling with rational proof and dispute with Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on depositary entrusting deposit to his household and apply the conditions that trigger liability.

Core Idea
A depositary may delegate to his household if trustworthy; necessity overrides owner's prohibition.

Can you leave your valuable deposit with the depositary's wife or children? The Hanafi school says yes, within limits. This passage from Marghinani's Hidayah explains the ruling, the Shafi'i objection, and the rational proofs that support the Hanafi position. Understanding this rule shows how necessity (darura) and custom shape liability in contracts of trust.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the depositary may keep the deposit with his own household (wife, children, parents, employee) without the owner's permission.
This is the default ruling, but it is conditional on the household's trustworthiness.
2
Identify the opposing view
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees: he requires the owner's explicit consent to delegate.
The Shafi'i proof is based on the owner's consent being specific to the depositary.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is rational (dalil aqli): (a) the depositary must safeguard as he safeguards his own property – and he uses his household for his own property; (b) practically, he cannot stay home always or carry the deposit everywhere. Necessity makes delegation permissible.
The proof does not rely on a specific text but on reason and custom.
4
Apply the conditions from the fatwa
The delegation is only to trustworthy household members. If the household is not trustworthy, he must not delegate. However, if the owner forbids delegation but the depositary has no alternative (e.g., must go out), the prohibition is ignored; if he has an alternative and still delegates, he is liable for loss.
Note the nuance: the fatwa limits delegation to household, not outsiders.
5
Distinguish the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Hanafi, if the trusted household loses the deposit without negligence, the depositary is not liable; under Shafi'i, he is liable unless the owner authorized the delegation.
This affects real cases of loss during the depositary's absence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وله حفظها بنفسه وعياله
[١] قوله : وله حفظها بنفسه وعياله ؛ يعني وللمودَع حفظ الوديعة بنفسه وبمن في عياله، وقال الشافعي : ليس للمودع أن يدفعها إلى من في عياله ؛ لأن مالكها رضي بحفظه لا بحفظ غيره. ولنا : إنَّ الواجب عليه أن يحفظها حفظ مال نفسه، وهو يحفظ بعياله ؛ ولأنَّ المودع لا يمكنه ملازمة بيته لحفظ الوديعة، ولا استصحابها في خروجه، فلم يكن له بد من حفظها بمن في عياله، وذكر السُّمُني (۲) نقلاً عن «الذخيرة»: إنَّ الدفع إلى من في العيال إنما يجوز إذا كان أمينا ، ولو دفعها المودعُ إلى أمين من أمنائه ليس في عياله لم يجز، وعليه الفتوى. انتهى.
فالحاصل : إن له حفظها بمن في عياله، وإن نهى المالك عن حفظها بهم، فإن هذا النهي غير معتد به ؛ لأنه لا يمكنه ملازمة بيته ولا استصحاب الوديعة في خروجه كما ذكرنا ، وذكر البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي»: نهاه عن الدفع إلى من في عياله فإن كان يجد بداً . من الدفع فدفع، وضاعت ضَمِنَ ، وإن لم يَجِد بُداً لا يضمن. انتهى. [٢]قوله : وعياله ؛ أي وبعياله الأمناء، وإنّما قيدنا به ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن به العيالُ أمناء لا يدفع إليها، صرّح به في المعدن» وغيره، والعيال : جمع عيل، يقال: عالَ الرجلُ اليتيم عولاً من باب قال كَفَلَهُ وَقَامَ به. كذا في «المصباح» (۳). وفيه أيضاً، والعيالُ : أهل البيت ومن يمونه الإنسانُ الواحد عَيْلٌ مِثلُ حِيَادٍ ، وجيد. انتهى. والمراد بالعيال هاهنا زوجة المودع، وولده ووالداه وأجيره.
And he may keep it with himself and his household. [1] His statement: 'he may keep it with himself and his household'; meaning the depositary may keep the deposit by himself and with those in his household. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for the depositary to give it to those in his household because the owner consented to his custody, not to the custody of others. Our proof: He is obligated to safeguard it as he safeguards his own property, and he safeguards his property with his household; and because the depositary cannot stay in his house to guard the deposit, nor can he carry it with him when he goes out, so he has no alternative but to safeguard it with his household. Al-Sumunī narrated from al-Dhakhīra: 'Giving to the household is only permitted if they are trustworthy; if the depositary gives it to a trustworthy person not of his household, it is not permitted, and that is the fatwa.' End. The conclusion: He may keep it with his household, and if the owner forbids him, that prohibition is not considered because he cannot stay home or take it out. Al-Birjandī narrated from Sharh al-Tahāwī: If he forbids giving to the household and he has an alternative and gives, and it is lost, he must compensate; if he has no alternative, he does not compensate. [2] His statement: 'and his household' meaning his trustworthy household; we restrict it because if the household are not trustworthy, he should not give it to them, as stated in al-Ma'din etc. 'Ayal' is plural of 'ayl'; it is said 'ala al-rajul al-yateem' meaning he supported him. In al-Misbah: 'Ayal' means the people of the house and those whom one supports; the singular is 'ayl'. Here 'ayal' means the depositary's wife, children, parents, and employee.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage addresses whether the depositary (al-mudi') may entrust the deposit to his household members (wife, children, parents, employee) for safekeeping. The Hanafi position affirms this right, while Imam al-Shafi'i prohibits it unless the owner explicitly consents. The Hanafi proof is based on two rational arguments: (1) the depositary is required to safeguard the deposit as he safeguards his own property, and he naturally entrusts his own property to his household; (2) practically, the depositary cannot remain at home continuously nor carry the deposit everywhere, so delegation to household is necessary. The commentator cites al-Sumunī from al-Dhakhīra that this permission is conditional on the household's trustworthiness; giving to a trustworthy outsider (not household) is not permitted according to the fatwa. If the owner explicitly forbids delegation, the prohibition is not binding if the depositary has no alternative (e.g., cannot stay home); but if he has an alternative and delegates anyway, he is liable for loss. This is from al-Birjandī's narration of Sharh al-Tahāwī. The term 'āyāl' is defined linguistically as dependents, and in this context means specifically the depositary's wife, children, parents, and hired employee. The passage also implicitly addresses a view of Imam Malik (referenced earlier but not in this page) regarding theft without loss of the depositary's own property; that is not directly discussed here.

Key Terms
العيال (al-'ayal): The depositary's dependents: wife, children, parents, and hired employee.
الحفظ (al-hifz): Safeguarding the deposit as one would his own property; a duty but not strict liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa cited in this passage, to whom may the depositary delegate the deposit?
A) Any trustworthy person, whether household or not.
B) Only to his household, if they are trustworthy.
C) Only with the owner's written permission.
D) Only to his wife, not to children or employees.

Key Takeaway
A depositary may entrust the deposit to his trustworthy household members, and necessity overrides the owner's prohibition against delegation.

Depositary's Right to Keep Deposit with Household
Ruling on depositary's right to use household for safekeeping, with dispute against Shafi'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on entrusting a deposit to household members, identify the Shafi'i objection, and evaluate the Hanafi response based on necessity and customary practice.

Core Idea
A depositary may entrust the deposit to trustworthy household members because he keeps his own property that way and cannot always stay home.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya, we examine a classic dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: may a depositary entrust the entrusted property to members of his household? The Hanafi position, rooted in practical necessity and analogical reasoning from one's own property, permits it. The passage also refines the ruling with conditions of trustworthiness and the impact of the owner's prohibition.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the permissibility for the depositary to keep the deposit with his household (wife, children, parents, hired servant living with him). Note that the criterion is cohabitation, not financial support.
The Arabic term ‘iyāl includes all who live with the depositary.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the owner agreed only to the depositary's personal custody, so delegation to household is a violation.
The objection is based on the specific agreement of the parties.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof
Analyze the two Hanafi proofs: (1) the depositary must keep it as his own property, and he naturally keeps his property with his household; (2) practical necessity — he cannot stay home always nor carry the deposit everywhere.
These proofs are analogical (qiyas) and considerations of necessity (darura).
4
Analyze the response
The Hanafi response explains that the depositary's custody is custodial, not physical; using trustworthy household members fulfills the obligation without negating responsibility.
The response also notes that even if the owner prohibits, the prohibition is generally ineffective due to necessity, unless the depositary has a clear alternative.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Shafi'i, entrusting to household renders the depositary liable for loss; under Hanafi, he is not liable if household members are trustworthy.
This difference affects real-world practice of safekeeping.

[١]قوله : وله حفظها بنفسه وعياله ؛ يعني وللمودَع حفظ الوديعة بنفسه وبمن في عياله، وقال الشافعي : ليس للمودع أن يدفعها إلى من في عياله ؛ لأن مالكها رضي بحفظه لا بحفظ غيره. ولنا : إنَّ الواجب عليه أن يحفظها حفظ مال نفسه، وهو يحفظ بعياله ؛ ولأنَّ المودع لا يمكنه ملازمة بيته لحفظ الوديعة، ولا استصحابها في خروجه، فلم يكن له بد من حفظها بمن في عياله، وذكر السُّمُني (۲) نقلاً عن «الذخيرة»: إنَّ الدفع إلى من في العيال إنما يجوز إذا كان أمينا ، ولو دفعها المودعُ إلى أمين من أمنائه ليس في عياله لم يجز، وعليه الفتوى. انتهى. و فإن هذا فالحاصل : إن له حفظها بمن في عياله، وإن نهى المالك عن حفظها بهم، النهي غير معتد به ؛ لأنه لا يمكنه ملازمة بيته ولا استصحاب الوديعة في خروجه كما ذكرنا ، وذكر البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي»: نهاه عن الدفع إلى من في عياله فإن كان يجد بداً . من الدفع فدفع، وضاعت ضَمِنَ ، وإن لم يَجِد بُداً لا يضمن. انتهى. [٢]قوله : وعياله ؛ أي وبعياله الأمناء، وإنّما قيدنا به ؛ لأنه لو لم يكن به العيالُ أمناء لا يدفع إليها، صرّح به في المعدن» وغيره، والعيال : جمع عيل، يقال: عالَ الرجلُ اليتيم عولاً من باب قال كَفَلَهُ وَقَامَ به. كذا في «المصباح» (۳). وفيه أيضاً، والعيالُ : أهل البيت ومن يمونه الإنسانُ الواحد عَيْلٌ مِثلُ حِيَادٍ ، وجيد. انتهى. والمراد بالعيال هاهنا زوجة المودع، وولده ووالداه وأجيره.
وقال في «الكفاية ) : المراد بالأجير هو التلميذ الخاص الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مسانهة، فأما الأجير بعمل من الأعمال فكسائر الأجانب يضمنُ بالدفع إليه. انتهى. والعبرة في هذا الباب للمساكنة لا للنفقة حتى لو دفعت المرأة وديعتها إلى زوجها لا يضمن إن هلكت ؛ لأنه ساكن معها، وحتى أنّ الابن الكبير للمودع إذا كان يسكن معه ولم يكن في نفقته ، فخرج الأب وترك المنزلُ على الابن يضمن الوديعة إن هلكت. وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز» (۳) : وعن محمد إنَّ المودَعَ إذا دفع الوديعة إلى وكيله وليس في عياله يضمن، أو دفع إلى أمين من أمنائه من يثقُ به في ماله، وليس في عياله لا يضمن ؛ لأنه حفظه مثل ما يحفظ ،ماله وجعله مثله، ولا يجب عليه أكثر من ذلك. ذكره في «النهاية»، ثم قال : وعليه الفتوى، وعزاه إلى التُمُرْتَاشِي، وهو إلى الحَلْوَانِي ثم قال : وعن هذا لم يشترط في «التحفة» في حفظ الوديعة بالعيال، فقال : ويلزمُ المودع حفظه إذا قبل الوديعة على وجه الذي يحفظ ماله. انتهى. وفي «الوَلْوَالَجية»: رجل أجر بيتاً من داره إنساناً، ودفع الوديعة إلى هذا المستأجر ، إن كان لكل واحد منهما غلق على حدة يضمن ؛ لأنه ليس في عياله، ولا بمنزلة من في عياله، وإن لم يكن لكل منهما غلق على حدة، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما يدخلُ على صاحبه بغير حشمة لا يضمن ؛ لأنه بمنزلة من في عياله. انتهى. فالحاصل أن العبرة للمسألة فينبغي أن لا يضمن بالدفع إلى أجنبي يسكن معه. ذكره مفيد يسعد وغيره.
And he has the right to keep it with himself and his household. And indeed the depositary is a volunteer in keeping it for its owner, and volunteering does not impose liability on the volunteer, for if it did, people would refrain. And in that is the disruption of their interests. And indeed the hand of the depositary is like the hand of the owner, so its destruction in his hand is like its destruction in the hand of the owner, so he is not liable for destruction. Malik said: If the deposit is stolen from the depositary and nothing of his own is stolen with it, he is not liable due to suspicion, as mentioned by al-Shimnī and others. [1] His statement: 'And he has the right to keep it with himself and his household' means the depositary has the right to keep the deposit with himself and with those who are in his household. Al-Shafi'i said: It is not permissible for the depositary to entrust it to those in his household because the owner agreed to his keeping, not the keeping of others. Our evidence: Indeed, what is obligatory upon him is to keep it as he keeps his own property, and he keeps his property with his household; and because the depositary cannot constantly stay at home to keep the deposit, nor can he take it with him when he goes out, so he has no alternative but to keep it through those in his household. Al-Sumnī narrated from al-Dhakhīra: Entrusting it to those in the household is only permissible if they are trustworthy. If the depositary entrusts it to a trustworthy person among his trustees who is not in his household, it is not permissible, and upon that is the fatwa. End. So the conclusion: He has the right to keep it through those in his household, and if the owner prohibits him from keeping it through them, the prohibition is not considered, because he cannot constantly stay at home nor take the deposit with him when going out, as we mentioned. Al-Burjandī narrated from Sharh al-Tahāwī: If he prohibited him from entrusting it to those in his household, and if he finds an alternative to entrusting but does so and it is lost, he is liable; if he has no alternative, he is not liable. End. [2] His statement: 'And his household' means his trustworthy household. We only qualified it with this because if the household members are not trustworthy, he should not entrust it to them, as stated in al-Ma'dan and others. 'al-‘Iyāl' is the plural of '‘ayl'. It is said: '‘āla al-rajulu al-yatīma ‘awlan' from the paradigm of 'qāla', meaning he supported and maintained him. This is in al-Misbāh. And in it also: al-‘Iyāl are the people of the house and those whom one supports. The singular is ‘ayl, like ḥiyād and jayyid. End. And the intended meaning of ‘iyāl here is the depositary's wife, his children, his parents, and his hired servant. Al-Kifāya said: The intended meaning of hired servant is the special student whom he hired by the month or by the year. As for a hired servant for a specific job, he is like any stranger; if he entrusts it to him, he is liable. End. And the criterion in this chapter is cohabitation, not financial support. Thus, if a woman entrusts her deposit to her husband, he is not liable if it is lost because he lives with her. Likewise, if the adult son of the depositary lives with him but is not under his financial support, and the father goes out leaving the home to the son, the son is liable for the deposit if it is lost. Al-Zayla‘ī said in Sharh al-Kanz: From Muhammad: If the depositary gives the deposit to his agent who is not in his household, he is liable. If he gives it to a trustworthy person among his trustees whom he trusts with his property, and he is not in his household, he is not liable because he has kept it as he keeps his own property and made it like it, and he is not obligated to do more than that. Mentioned in al-Nihāya, then he said: Upon that is the fatwa, and he attributed it to al-Tamurtāshī, then to al-Ḥalwānī. Then he said: Because of this, al-Tuḥfa did not stipulate [keeping] through household in the preservation of the deposit; he said: The depositary is obligated to preserve it when he accepts the deposit in the manner he preserves his own property. End. In al-Walwālijiyya: A man rented a room in his house to a person, and gave the deposit to this tenant. If each of them has a separate lock, he is liable because he is not in his household nor like one in his household. If each does not have a separate lock, and each enters upon the other without formality, he is not liable because he is like one in his household. End. So the conclusion is that the criterion for the matter is that he should not be liable for entrusting it to a stranger who lives with him. Mentioned by Mufīd Yasa‘ad and others.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the Hanafi ruling that a depositary (someone entrusted with a deposit) may keep the deposit with himself and his household. The commentary begins by stating the general principle: the depositary is a volunteer, and thus not liable for loss unless negligent. The hand of the depositary is like the hand of the owner, so loss is like loss in the owner's hand. A minority view of Imam Malik is mentioned briefly and rejected. The main dispute is with Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that the depositary cannot entrust the deposit to household members because the owner only agreed to the depositary's personal custody. The Hanafi response is twofold: (1) the depositary is only required to keep it as he keeps his own property, and he naturally keeps his property with his household; (2) practical necessity — the depositary cannot stay home all the time or carry the deposit everywhere. The commentary then qualifies that the household members must be trustworthy (amīn). If the owner explicitly prohibits entrusting to household, that prohibition is generally ignored due to necessity, but if the depositary has a viable alternative (e.g., he can easily keep it himself), then entrusting it would make him liable if lost. The term 'iyāl (household) is defined: wife, children, parents, and a hired servant (specifically a general hired helper like a student, not a specific job worker). The key criterion is cohabitation (musākana), not financial support. Several case examples from later Hanafi authorities illustrate when entrusting to a co-resident stranger does not incur liability. Overall, the Hanafi position is that the depositary is not liable for loss when he keeps the deposit with trustworthy household members, even if the owner prohibits it, provided he has no reasonable alternative.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī‘ah): A property entrusted to someone for safekeeping without compensation.
المودع (al-mūdi‘): The person who entrusts the deposit (the depositor).
العيال (al-‘iyāl): Household members including wife, children, parents, and a hired servant who lives with the depositary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a depositary liable for loss if he entrusts the deposit to his household?
A) Whenever he entrusts it, he is liable.
B) He is not liable if the household members are trustworthy, even if the owner prohibits it, provided he has no alternative.
C) He is liable only if the household member is a hired servant.
D) He is liable if the owner explicitly prohibits it, regardless of need.

Key Takeaway
The depositary may entrust the deposit to trustworthy household members without liability, based on necessity and analogy to his own property, even if the owner objects, as long as no alternative exists.

Depositary's Right to Travel with the Deposit
Ruling on depositary's right to travel with the deposit under safety conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for a depositary to travel with the deposit and apply them to a given scenario.

Core Idea
A depositary may travel with a deposit unless the owner forbids it or the journey is unsafe.

When a person entrusts property to another, the depositary is expected to safeguard it. But what if the depositary needs to travel? This lesson examines a clear ruling from the Hanafi school: the depositary may take the deposit on a journey, provided two conditions are met. The passage from a commentary on al-Hidayah lays out these conditions concisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act under consideration is the depositary traveling with the deposit. The ruling is that it is permissible (mubah) — neither obligatory nor prohibited by default.
The ruling is conditional, not absolute.
2
Extract the First Condition
The first condition is the absence of a prohibition from the owner of the deposit. If the owner explicitly forbids travel, the depositary may not do so.
This respects the owner's right to control the placement of their property.
3
Extract the Second Condition
The second condition is the absence of fear (khawf) — meaning the journey must be safe. The passage explains safety as: the road is safe, no one intends evil toward the deposit, and if someone does, the depositary can defend it himself or with companions.
Note: 'fear' is objective, not merely subjective anxiety.
4
Note the Additional Permissibility
The passage adds that travel is allowed even if the deposit is heavy or burdensome for the depositary. The weight does not change the ruling.
This confirms that the ruling is lenient toward the depositary.
5
Summarize the Madhhab Position
The commentator states that this is the madhhab of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). No intra-madhhab dispute is mentioned here.
Other schools may have differing conditions, but within Hanafi this is the relied-upon view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والسفور ...الخ ؛ يعني وللمودع السفر بالوديعة، وإن كان لها حمل ومؤنة عند عدم النهي من صاحب الوديعة، وعدم الخوف، بأن كان الطريق آمناً، لا يقصده فيه أحد بسوء، ولو قصده يمكنه دفعه بنفسه أو برفقته وهذا مذهب الإمام.
His statement: 'And traveling with it...' etc.; meaning the depositary has the right to travel with the deposit, even if it has weight and burden, in the absence of a prohibition from the owner of the deposit, and absence of fear, such that the road is safe, no one intends him with evil, and if someone intends him, he can repel him by himself or with his companions. And this is the madhhab of the Imam.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that a depositary may travel with the deposit under two conditions: (1) the owner has not prohibited it, and (2) there is no fear for its safety — meaning the road is safe, no one threatens the deposit, and if someone does, the depositary can defend it himself or with his companions. Even if the deposit is heavy or burdensome, it is permitted. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage does not mention any dispute; it is a straightforward ruling statement from the commentary.

Key Terms
السفور (al-sufūr): Traveling with the deposit; the act of taking it on a journey.
الخوف (al-khawf): Fear of loss or theft; if present, travel with deposit is not allowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition may the depositary travel with the deposit?
A) Only if the deposit is light.
B) Only if the owner explicitly allows it.
C) If the road is safe and the owner has not prohibited it.
D) He may always travel with it.

Key Takeaway
A depositary may travel with a deposit as long as the owner has not forbidden it and the journey is safe, even if the deposit is heavy.

Depositary Liability When Delivering Deposit to a Co-Resident
Ruling on depositary liability when delivering deposit to a co-resident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on depositary liability when delivering the deposit to a third party, identify the decisive factor (cohabitation vs. financial dependency), and apply it to practical scenarios involving tenants, spouses, and employees.

Core Idea
The depositary is liable for giving the deposit to a third party only if that third party does not live with him — cohabitation, not dependency, determines liability.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah clarifies a key nuance in the law of deposit (wadī‘ah). While the depositary is generally a trustee (amīn) not liable for loss without negligence, delivering the deposit to an outsider can breach the duty of care. The Hanafi school has a detailed framework: the decisive factor is whether the recipient lives with the depositary, not whether the depositary supports them financially. This lesson unpacks that principle through multiple examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The deposit is given by the depositary to a third party. The question is whether this makes the depositary liable if the deposit is destroyed.
2
Locate the decisive criterion
The text states 'al-‘ibrah li al-musākanah' (the consideration is cohabitation). This is the key principle.
Cohabitation means sharing the same dwelling, not merely being in the same household financially.
3
Apply to the examples: wife, son, tenant
Wife lives with husband → no liability. Tenant with separate lock → liable; without separate lock → no liability. Adult son living with father but not financially dependent, if father leaves the house to him, son becomes liable.
The adult son example is nuanced: cohabitation alone does not automatically absolve; additional factors like entrusting the house matter.
4
Differentiate between types of hired persons
A private employee (ajīr khāṣṣ) hired by month/year is like a household member; giving to him does not incur liability. A task worker (ajīr ‘āmm) is like any stranger; giving to him does incur liability.
This distinction comes from al-Kifayah as cited in the text.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon fatwa is that the depositary is not liable for giving the deposit to a co-resident stranger, as per the example of a tenant sharing a house without separate locks. This is upheld by al-Zayla‘i and al-Tumurtashi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة تعريفها وأحكامها
والسفور بها
(1)
وقال في «الكفاية» : المراد بالأجير هو التلميذ الخاص الذي استأجره مشاهرة أو مسانهة، فأما الأجير بعمل من الأعمال فكسائر الأجانب يضمنُ بالدفع إليه. انتهى. والعبرة في هذا الباب للمساكنة لا للنفقة حتى لو دفعت المرأة وديعتها إلى زوجها لا يضمن إن هلكت ؛ لأنه ساكن معها، وحتى أنّ الابن الكبير للمودع إذا كان يسكن معه ولم يكن في نفقته ، فخرج الأب وترك المنزلُ على الابن يضمن الوديعة إن هلكت. وقال الزيلعي في «شرح الكنز» (۳) : وعن محمد إنَّ المودَعَ إذا دفع الوديعة إلى وكيله وليس في عياله يضمن، أو دفع إلى أمين من أمنائه من يثقُ به في ماله، وليس في عياله لا يضمن ؛ لأنه حفظه مثل ما يحفظ ماله وجعله مثله، ولا يجب عليه أكثر من ذلك. ذكره في «النهاية»، ثم قال : وعليه الفتوى، وعزاه إلى التُمُرْتَاشِي، وهو إلى الحَلْوَانِي ثم قال : وعن هذا لم يشترط في «التحفة» في حفظ الوديعة بالعيال، فقال : ويلزمُ المودع حفظه إذا قبل الوديعة على وجه الذي يحفظ ماله. انتهى. وفي «الوَلْوَالَجية»: رجل أجر بيتاً من داره إنساناً، ودفع الوديعة إلى هذا المستأجر ، إن كان لكل واحد منهما غلق على حدة يضمن ؛ لأنه ليس في عياله، ولا بمنزلة من في عياله، وإن لم يكن لكل منهما غلق على حدة، وكلُّ واحدٍ منهما يدخلُ على صاحبه بغير حشمة لا يضمن ؛ لأنه بمنزلة من في عياله. انتهى. فالحاصل أن العبرة للمسألة فينبغي أن لا يضمن بالدفع إلى أجنبي يسكن معه.
ذكره مفيد يسعد وغيره.
The book of deposit: its definition and rulings. And traveling with it. (1) He said in al-Kifayah: What is meant by 'ajir is the private student whom he hires on a monthly or yearly basis. As for an ajir for a specific task, he is like other strangers: the depositary is liable if he gives it to him. End. The consideration in this chapter is cohabitation, not financial support: even if a woman gives her deposit to her husband, he is not liable if it is destroyed, because he lives with her. And even the adult son of the depositor, if he lives with him and is not under his support, and the father goes out and leaves the house to the son, the son is liable for the deposit if it is destroyed. Al-Zayla'i said in Sharh al-Kanz: From Muhammad: if the depositary gives the deposit to his agent and he is not in his household, he is liable; or if he gives it to a trusted person among his trustees whom he trusts with his property, and he is not in his household, he is not liable, because he preserved it as he preserves his own property and made it like his own, and nothing more is required of him. Mentioned in al-Nihayah, then he said: Upon this is the fatwa, and he attributed it to al-Tumurtashi, then to al-Halwani. Then he said: Based on this, al-Tuhfah did not stipulate preservation of the deposit specifically with household members; he said: The depositary must preserve it when he accepts the deposit in the manner he preserves his own property. End. In al-Walwalijiyyah: A man rented a room in his house to someone, and gave the deposit to this tenant. If each of them has a separate lock, he is liable, because he is not in his household, nor in the status of one in his household. If they do not have separate locks, and each enters upon the other without formality, he is not liable, because he is in the status of one in his household. End. So the conclusion is that the consideration in the matter is cohabitation, so he should not be liable for giving it to a stranger who lives with him. Mentioned by Mufid Yas'ud and others.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on al-Wiqayah regarding the depositary's liability when he hands the deposit to a third party. The key principle (al-‘ibrah) is cohabitation (musākanah), not financial dependence (nafaqah). The text first defines the term 'ajir (hired person) as a private student hired by month or year; whereas a hired laborer for a specific job is treated as a stranger, so giving the deposit to him makes the depositary liable if lost. Then it applies the principle: a wife giving the deposit to her husband—no liability because they live together; an adult son living with the father but not under his support—if the father leaves the house to the son, the son is liable because he is a co-resident, not a dependent. Al-Zayla‘i in Sharh al-Kanz reports from Imam Muhammad: if the depositary gives to his agent not in his household, he is liable; but if to a trusted person in his property matters, even if not in household, no liability, because he is preserving it as his own. The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is that the depositary is not liable for giving to a co-resident stranger, as illustrated by the Walwalijiyyah example of a tenant with or without separate locks.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī‘ah): A property placed with another for safekeeping without compensation.
المودَع (al-mūda‘): The person who accepts the deposit for safekeeping.
العبرة للمساكنة (al-‘ibrah li al-musākanah): The decisive factor is cohabitation, not financial dependence.
الأجير (al-ajīr): A hired person; here distinguished between a private employee (ajīr khāṣṣ) and a task worker (ajīr ‘āmm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key factor determining depositary liability when he delivers the deposit to a third party?
A) Whether the third party is financially dependent on the depositary
B) Whether the third party lives with the depositary
C) Whether the third party is a relative
D) Whether the third party is trustworthy

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: a depositary is not liable for delivering the deposit to someone who lives with him, even if that person is not financially dependent, because cohabitation is the decisive factor.

Travel with the Deposit: Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute with inter-madhhab comparisons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for traveling with a deposit and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A depositary may travel with a deposit on a safe road unless the owner forbids it.

Depositary travel is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute. Imam Abu Hanifa gives broad permission, while his two disciples impose restrictions based on burden and expense. Understanding their proofs sharpens your ability to trace rulings to usul principles.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
Identify the legal question: may a depositary (al-mūda‘) travel with the deposited property (wadī‘ah) without the owner's explicit permission?
2
Identify Abu Hanifa’s position and proof
Abu Hanifa permits traveling if the road is safe and the owner has not prohibited it. His proof: the command to preserve is absolute, not restricted by place, and the desert can be a place of preservation if safe.
He also analogizes to a guardian traveling with an orphan's property.
3
Identify the Sahibayn’s positions
Abu Yusuf allows only short journeys for burdensome deposits; Muhammad forbids altogether when heavy burden/expense. Their proofs: owner's presumed non-consent and risk of expense consuming the deposit.
Al-Hidayah aligns Abu Yusuf with Muhammad (Sahibayn) against Abu Hanifa.
4
Compare with other madhhabs
Al-Shafi‘i prohibits absolutely (customary preservation is in cities); Malik requires returning if possible.
This shows the spectrum of opinions.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'wa hādhā madhhab al-imām', indicating Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi school.
Conditions: safe road, no owner prohibition, even if heavy burden.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : والسفور ...الخ ؛ يعني وللمودع السفر بالوديعة، وإن كان لها حمل ومؤنة عند عدم النهي من صاحب الوديعة، وعدم الخوف، بأن كان الطريق آمناً، لا يقصده فيه أحد بسوء، ولو قصده يمكنه دفعه بنفسه أو برفقته وهذا مذهب الإمام.
وقال أبو يوسف له : له السفر بها إن كانت المسافة قصيرة، وإن كانت طويلة فليس له ذلك فيما له حمل ومؤنة ؛ لأنَّ القصيرة لا خوف منها عادة ؛ ولهذا تسافر المرأة فيها بغير زوج ولا محرم. وقال محمد : ليس له السفر بها فيما له حمل ومؤنة، والظاهر من حال صاحبها أنه لا يرضى به، وربما تستغرقُ المؤنة الوديعة، وفيه إهلاكها، فلا يجوز كالوكيل بالبيع، حيث لا يجوز له السفر بالبيع، وإن سافر به ضمن.
وقال الشافعي له : ليس له ذلك مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّ المطلق ينصرف إلى المتعارف ، وهو الحفظ في الأمصار دون المفازة، وصار كالاستحفاظ بأجر، فكما إذا استأجر رجلاً ليحفظ متاعه شهرا بدرهم فليس له السفر به، فإن سافر به ضمن فكذا هذا. وقال مالك : ليس له ذلك إذا قدر أن يردها على صاحبها أو وكيله أو الحاكم أو أمينه.
والدليل على ما ذهب إليه الإمام : إن أمره بالحفظ مطلق فلا يتقيد بالمكان، كما لا يتقيد بالزمان فإن قلت : إن إطلاق الأمر مسلم وهو يقتضي الجواز، لكن المانع عنه متحقق، وهو كون المفازة غير محلّ الحفظ.
قلنا : إنَّ المفازة محل للحفظ إذا كان الطريق آمناً، والكلام فيه فصار كالمصر؛ ولهذا يملكه الأب والوصي في مال الصغير، مع أن ولايتهما نظرية ؛ لقوله : ( وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا مَالَ الْيَتِيمِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ) (١) ، فلولا أنه من الأحسن لما جاز ذلك لهما (٢). ويفهم من «الهداية»: إنَّ أبا يوسف مع محمد ، حيث قال صاحب «الهداية»(۳) : وقالا : ليس له ذلك اذا كان لها حمل ومؤنة. انتهى. ودليلهما : إنَّ المالك يلزمه المؤنة حينئذ فيما له حمل ومؤنة، والظاهر أنّ المالك لا يرضى به ؛ فلا بد أن يقيد سفره بما ليس له حمل ومؤنة ؛ لأنَّ فيما له حمل ومؤنة إضرار عليه.
His saying: 'and traveling with it' etc.; meaning the depositary has the right to travel with the deposit, even if it has heavy burden and expense, provided there is no prohibition from the owner of the deposit and no fear (i.e., the road is safe, no one intends evil, and if someone does, he can repel him by himself or with his company). This is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa.
And Abu Yusuf said to him: He has the right to travel with it if the distance is short; if it is long, he does not have that right for deposits with heavy burden and expense, because a short journey is not normally feared; for this reason a woman travels on such a journey without husband or mahram. And Muhammad said: He does not have the right to travel with it if it has heavy burden and expense, and the apparent position of the owner is that he would not consent to it; perhaps the expense would consume the deposit, and that is destruction of it, so it is not permissible, like an agent for sale, who is not allowed to travel with the goods; if he travels, he is liable.
And al-Shafi'i said to him: He does not have that right absolutely, because the absolute command turns to the customary, which is preservation in cities, not in the wilderness; it becomes like preservation for hire: just as if one hires a man to preserve his goods for a month for a dirham, he may not travel with them, and if he travels, he is liable, so also here. And Malik said: He does not have that right if he is able to return it to its owner or his agent or the judge or his trustee.
The proof for what Imam Abu Hanifa went to: The command to preserve is absolute, so it is not restricted by place, just as it is not restricted by time. If you say: The absoluteness of the command is accepted, and it implies permissibility, but the impediment is present, namely that the wilderness is not a place of preservation.
We say: The wilderness is a place of preservation if the road is safe, and the discussion is about that, so it becomes like a city; for this reason the father and guardian may do so with the property of a minor, even though their authority is based on consideration, because of His saying: 'And do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner' (6:152); were it not that it is of the best, it would not be permissible for them. And from al-Hidayah it is understood that Abu Yusuf is with Muhammad, as the author of al-Hidayah said: 'And both said: He does not have that right if it has heavy burden and expense.' End. And their proof: The owner would then bear the expense for something with heavy burden and expense, and it is apparent that the owner would not consent to it; so he must restrict his travel to what has no heavy burden and expense, because traveling with heavy burden and expense causes harm to him.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples) and also cites the positions of al-Shafi‘i and Malik. The issue is whether the depositary may travel with the deposit. Abu Hanifa permits it unconditionally as long as the road is safe and the owner has not prohibited it; the command to preserve is absolute like a guardian's authority over an orphan's property. Abu Yusuf restricts permission to short journeys only, because short journeys are not typically dangerous. Muhammad prohibits it entirely when the deposit has heavy burden and expense, reasoning that the owner would not consent and the expense might consume the deposit, analogous to an agent for sale who cannot travel with the goods. The Shafi‘i view prohibits absolutely, as preservation is customarily in cities. The Maliki view requires returning the deposit if possible. The commentary then provides the dalil for Abu Hanifa: the preservation command is unrestricted, and the desert can be safe like a city, citing the verse about orphans. It also notes that al-Hidayah aligns Abu Yusuf with Muhammad (Sahibayn) against Abu Hanifa on this point. The relied-upon position (al-mustaftā bihi) in the Hanafi madhhab is Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated by 'wa hādhā madhhab al-imām'.

Key Terms
المودع (al-mūda‘): The depositary (the person keeping the deposit).
السفر بالوديعة (al-safar bi al-wadī‘ah): Traveling with the deposited property.
الحمل والمؤنة (al-ḥaml wa al-mu’nah): Heavy burden and expense associated with the deposit.
المفازة (al-mafāzah): Desert or uninhabited area, a place of potential danger.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when may a depositary travel with a deposit?
A) Only if the owner explicitly permits.
B) If the road is safe and the owner has not prohibited it.
C) Only if the deposit has no heavy burden.
D) Never, because the deposit must stay in the city.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (mufta bihi) allows a depositary to travel with a deposit if the road is safe and the owner has not prohibited travel, regardless of heavy burden.
