Traveling with a Deposit: Hanafi Dispute and Mufta Bihi Ruling
Dispute on permissibility of depositary traveling with deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on traveling with a deposit, apply the mufta bihi ruling, and evaluate the Shafi'i and Maliki counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Travel with a deposit is permitted unless it imposes burden; the Sahibayn prohibit it when weight and expense exist.

This lesson examines a key dispute in Hanafi fiqh: may a depositary (mudi') travel with the deposited item (wadi'ah)? The issue pits Abu Hanifa's broad permission against the Sahibayn's restriction when the deposit has weight and expense. We will also see how the Hanafi school responds to the absolute prohibition of al-Shafi'i and the conditional prohibition of Malik, and identify the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the three Hanafi positions: Abu Hanifa (permissible unless prohibited or fear), Abu Yusuf (short journeys permissible, long only if no weight/expense), Muhammad (prohibited if weight/expense). Note the agreement of the Sahibayn in al-Hidayah.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on prohibition when the deposit has weight and expense.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their proofs
State the Shafi'i position (absolute prohibition based on customary practice and analogy to hired safekeeping) and the Maliki position (prohibition if the depositary can return the deposit).
Shafi'i's analogy to ijarah is central; Maliki focuses on the ability to return.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Examine the absolute command to safeguard (Quran 6:152) and the argument that the desert can be a place of safekeeping if safe. Understand why Abu Hanifa sees no restriction by place.
The verse about orphan property supports that travel can be beneficial.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to other schools
Understand why the Hanafi school rejects the Shafi'i analogy (deposit is voluntary, not contractual) and the Maliki condition (depositary has right to travel unless prohibited).
The key distinction: deposit is mu'awanah (assistance), not mu'awadah (exchange).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Identify the practical consequences: under the Sahibayn's view, traveling with a heavy deposit makes the depositary liable; under Shafi'i, any travel does; under Maliki, only if return was possible. The mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's position.
The relied-upon ruling is that travel is prohibited if the deposit has weight and expense, as stated in al-Hidayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو يوسف له : له السفر بها إن كانت المسافة قصيرة، وإن كانت طويلة فليس له ذلك فيما له حمل ومؤنة ؛ لأنَّ القصيرة لا خوف منها عادة ؛ ولهذا تسافر المرأة فيها بغير زوج ولا محرم. وقال محمد : ليس له السفر بها فيما له حمل ومؤنة، والظاهر من حال صاحبها أنه لا يرضى به، وربما تستغرقُ المؤنة الوديعة، وفيه إهلاكها، فلا يجوز كالوكيل بالبيع، حيث لا يجوز له السفر بالبيع، وإن سافر به ضمن.
وقال الشافعي له : ليس له ذلك مطلقاً ؛ لأنَّ المطلق ينصرف إلى المتعارف ، وهو الحفظ في الأمصار دون المفازة، وصار كالاستحفاظ بأجر، فكما إذا استأجر رجلاً ليحفظ متاعه شهرا بدرهم فليس له السفر به، فإن سافر به ضَمِن فكذا هذا. وقال مالك : ليس له ذلك إذا قدر أن يردها على صاحبها أو وكيله أو الحاكم أو أمينه.
والدليل على ما ذهب إليه الإمام الله : إن أمره بالحفظ مطلق فلا يتقيد بالمكان، كما لا يتقيد بالزمان فإن قلت : إن إطلاق الأمر مسلم وهو يقتضي الجواز، لكن المانع عنه متحقق، وهو كون المفازة غير محلّ الحفظ.
قلنا : إنَّ المفازة محل للحفظ إذا كان الطريق آمناً، والكلام فيه فصار كالمصر؛ ولهذا يملكه الأب والوصي في مال الصغير، مع أن ولايتهما نظرية ؛ لقوله : ( وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا مَالَ الْيَتِيمِ إِلَّا بِالَّتِي هِيَ أَحْسَنُ ) ، فلولا أنه من الأحسن لما جاز ذلك لهما. ويفهم من «الهداية»: إنَّ أبا يوسف مع محمد ، حيث قال صاحب «الهداية» : وقالا : ليس له ذلك اذا كان لها حمل ومؤنة. انتهى. ودليلهما : إنَّ المالكَ يلزمه المؤنة حينئذ فيما له حمل ومؤنة، والظاهر أنّ المالك لا يرضى به ؛ فلا بد أن يقيد سفره بما ليس له حمل ومؤنة ؛ لأنَّ فيما له حمل ومؤنة إضرار عليه.
عند عدم النهي والخوف) : السُّفُور": الخروج للسفر، فالسفور) مصدر، والسفر الحاصل بالمصدر، فاختار المصدر
والجواب عن هذا الاستدلال : إنَّ مؤنة الردّ يلزم المالك في ملكه ، لكن ليس لمعنى من قبل المودع ، بل ضرورة امتثال أمره ، فلا يبالي به ؛ لكونه ضرورياً وضمنياً. والجواب عما استدل به الشافعي له : إنَّ المعتاد كون المودعين وقت الإيداع في المصر لا حفظهم ، فإنّ من كان في المفازة يحفظ ماله فيها، ولا ينقله إلى الأمصار، والقياس على الاستحفاظ بأجر قياس مع الفارق ؛ لأن الاستحفاظ بأجر هو عقد معاوضة، فإنه بالإجارة اشترى منافعه، فالحفظ إنّما يقع بمنافع الملك فيلزمه المقام مع المالك ليمكنه التسليم كما وجب عليه ؛ لأنَّ الداخل تحت العقد هو المنافع في المصر، فإذا خرج صارَ مخالفاً ؛ لأنَّ هذه المنافع غير داخلةٍ فضَمِنَ.
بخلاف ما نحن فيه، فإنَّ المودَعَ لا يحفظ إلا على سبيل المعونة، فلا يحجر به ، وهذا إذا لم يعيّن المالك المصر للحفظ فيه، بل أطلق الحفظ، فإن عين المصر للحفظ فيه فسافر، فإن كان سفراً له بد منه ضمن ، وإلا فإن أمكنه الحفظ في المصر مع السفر، بإن كان ترك الوديعة عند عياله في المصر ضمن وإلا لا.
Abu Yusuf said: He may travel with it if the distance is short, but if it is long then he may not do so if it has weight and expense, because short journeys are not usually feared; hence a woman travels on such a journey without a husband or mahram. Muhammad said: He may not travel with it if it has weight and expense, and the apparent situation of its owner is that he would not consent to it, and perhaps the expense would consume the deposit, and therein is destruction of it, so it is impermissible like the agent for sale, where he may not travel with the goods, and if he travels with them he is liable. Al-Shafi'i said: He has no right to do so absolutely, because the absolute (command) is directed to what is customary, which is safekeeping in towns not in the desert, and it becomes like a hired safekeeping; just as if a man hires a person to safeguard his goods for a month for a dirham, he may not travel with them, and if he travels with them he is liable, so also this. Malik said: He may not do so if he is able to return it to its owner or his agent or the judge or his trustee. The proof for what Imam (Abu Hanifa) held is that his command to safeguard is absolute and is not restricted by place, just as it is not restricted by time. If you say: The absoluteness of the command is granted and it implies permissibility, but the preventing factor is established, namely that the desert is not a place for safekeeping. We say: The desert is a place for safekeeping if the road is safe, and the discourse concerns that, so it becomes like a town; hence the father and guardian have authority over the orphan's property, even though their guardianship is supervisory, due to His statement: 'And do not approach the property of the orphan except in the best manner' (6:152). Were it not that it is among the best things, it would not be permissible for them. It is understood from al-Hidayah that Abu Yusuf is with Muhammad, as the author of al-Hidayah said: The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: He may not do so if it has weight and expense. End quote. Their proof is that the owner is then burdened with the expense if it has weight and expense, and it is apparent that the owner does not consent to that; so his travel must be restricted to what has no weight and expense, because what has weight and expense causes harm to him. (Condition: when there is no prohibition and no fear) The word 'sufur' means setting out on a journey; so 'sufur' is the verbal noun, and the journey is the result of the verbal noun, so he chose the verbal noun. The response to this inference is that the expense of return is incumbent upon the owner in his property, but not for a reason from the depositary, rather out of necessity to comply with his command, so he is not concerned with it because it is necessary and implicit. The response to what al-Shafi'i inferred is that the customary practice of depositors at the time of deposit is in the town, not their safeguarding; for one who is in the desert safeguards his property there and does not transfer it to towns. The analogy to hired safekeeping is an analogy with a difference, because hired safekeeping is a contract of exchange, for by hiring one purchases the benefits, so safeguarding occurs through the benefits of the property, so he must stay with the owner to enable delivery as required, because what falls under the contract is the benefits in the town, so if he leaves he becomes violative because these benefits are not included and thus he is liable. Unlike our case, for the depositary safeguards only by way of assistance, so he is not restricted. And this is if the owner did not specify the town for safeguarding, but made the safeguarding absolute. If he specified the town for safeguarding and then traveled, then if the travel is unavoidable he is liable; otherwise, if he can safeguard in the town while traveling, for example if he leaves the deposit with his family in the town, he is liable, otherwise not.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, discusses the ruling of a depositary (mudi') traveling with a deposit (wadi'ah). The text presents the views of the major Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) as well as the positions of Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Malik. The core issue is whether the depositary is permitted to take the deposit on a journey, given that the owner entrusted it for safekeeping. Abu Hanifa holds that the absolute command to safeguard does not restrict place, so travel is permitted unless the owner explicitly prohibits it or the journey is unsafe. Abu Yusuf distinguishes between short and long journeys: short ones are permissible, long ones only if the deposit has no weight or expense, because otherwise the owner would not consent. Muhammad al-Shaybani prohibits travel with the deposit if it has weight and expense, analogizing to an agent for sale who may not travel with goods. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) agree on this restriction according to al-Hidayah. Al-Shafi'i absolutely prohibits travel with the deposit, arguing that the absolute command refers to customary safekeeping in towns, like a hired keeper. Malik restricts it to cases where the depositary cannot return the deposit to the owner or his representative. The Hanafi response to Shafi'i is that the analogy to hired safekeeping fails because hired safekeeping is a contract of exchange (ijarah) where specific benefits are contracted, while the depositary merely assists voluntarily. The text also covers the case where the owner specifies a town for safekeeping: if the depositary travels without leaving the deposit in that town, he is liable. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, restricting travel when the deposit has weight and expense, as understood from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): The deposit or entrusted property that a person leaves with another for safekeeping.
السفر (al-safar): Travel or journey, specifically leaving one's town to another place. In this context, the depositary taking the deposit on a journey.
المؤنة (al-mu'nah): Expense or burden, including cost and effort involved in transporting or maintaining the deposit during travel.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, when is it NOT permissible for a depositary to travel with the deposit?
A) When the journey is long.
B) When the deposit has weight and expense, and the owner's consent is not apparent.
C) When the depositary has no guardian.
D) When the owner has not explicitly given permission.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position prohibits traveling with a deposit that has weight and expense, as the Sahibayn hold, to prevent harm to the owner.

beginner

Ruling on the Depositary Traveling with the Deposit
Explains when a depositary may travel with the deposit and liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary (mudi‘) may travel with the deposit and identify when he becomes liable (damin).

Core Idea
A depositary may only travel with the deposit without prohibition and without danger; otherwise he is liable.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the Hanafi ruling on a depositary traveling with a deposit. It draws from al-Hidayah and clarifies that the depositary is not free to travel arbitrarily; his permission is conditional on the owner's consent and the safety of the route. The commentary also refutes an alternative view attributed to Imam al-Shafi'i, distinguishing deposit from hired custody.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling
State the basic rule: the depositary may travel with the deposit only when there is no prohibition from the owner and no fear (danger) on the route.
The text explicitly mentions these two conditions.
2
Understand the Shafi'i Objection and Hanafi Response
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i reportedly analogized deposit to hired preservation (istihfaz bi-ajr), arguing that the depositary must remain in the city. The Hanafi response explains that hired preservation is a commutative contract (ijarah), whereas deposit is gratuitous assistance, so the analogy fails.
The commentary highlights this distinction to support the Hanafi view.
3
Distinguish Cases of City Specification
If the owner specified a city for preservation, traveling outside that city makes the depositary liable unless the travel is unavoidable or he leaves the deposit with his family in the city.
This is a further condition beyond the general rule.
4
Determine Liability
Liability arises when the depositary violates a condition: if the owner prohibited travel, or the route is dangerous, or he travels despite a specified city without excuse, and the deposit is lost, he must compensate.
The ruling is from al-Hidayah, part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

The book of deposit: its definition and rulings. When there is no prohibition and no fear. 'When there is no prohibition and fear': as-sufur means going out for travel, so as-sufur is a verbal noun, and travel is the result of the verbal noun, so he chose the verbal noun. And the answer to this argument: Indeed, the burden of returning is incumbent upon the owner in his property, but not for a meaning from the depositary's side, rather out of necessity to comply with his command, so he does not care about it because it is necessary and implicit. And the answer to what al-Shafi'i argued: Indeed, it is customary that depositors at the time of deposit are in the city, not their preservation, for one who is in the wilderness preserves his property there and does not transport it to the cities. And analogy to hired preservation is a flawed analogy because hired preservation is a contract of exchange, for through ijarah he purchased his benefits, so preservation occurs through the benefits of the property, so he must remain with the owner to enable delivery as required, because what falls under the contract is the benefits in the city, so if he leaves, he becomes contravening because these benefits are not included, so he is liable. Unlike our case, for the depositary preserves only by way of assistance, so he is not restricted. And this is if the owner did not specify the city for preservation, but rather left the preservation absolute. If he specified the city for preservation and then traveled, if the travel is one from which he can desist, he is liable; otherwise, if he can preserve in the city while traveling, such as if he left the deposit with his family in the city, he is liable, otherwise not. He (the commentator) said: 'When there is no prohibition' is a condition; it is conditioned by the absence of prohibition and absence of fear, because the depositary does not have the right to travel with the deposit if its owner prohibits him, without disagreement among the scholars. And if he is prohibited from travel, or the road is dangerous, and he travels and the property is lost, he is liable. He said: 'He is liable' because the restriction is beneficial, for preservation in the city is more effective, so it is valid. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary from the Hanafi school regarding when a depositary (al-mudi‘) may travel with the deposited item (al-wadi‘ah). The base ruling from the matn stipulates that the depositary may travel only when there is no prohibition from the owner and no fear (danger). The commentary expands on this, addressing an objection about the owner bearing the cost of returning the deposit: that cost is incidental and does not affect the ruling. It also refutes the Shafi‘i analogy to hired preservation (istihfaz bi-ajr), explaining that hired preservation is a commutative contract requiring the hired party to remain in the city, whereas deposit is gratuitous assistance. The passage then details the case where the owner specifies the city for preservation: if the depositary travels, he is liable unless the travel is unavoidable or he leaves the deposit with his family in the city. The ruling is emphatic: if the owner prohibits travel or the route is dangerous, and the depositary travels and the property is lost, he is liable (damin). The reasoning is that preservation in the city is more effective. This ruling is taken from al-Hidayah and represents the Hanafi position without intra-madhhab dispute here.

Key Terms
سفور (sufūr): Going out for travel; the verbal noun (masdar) meaning travel.
مصر (miṣr): A city or settled area; the place where the deposit is normally kept.
ضمن (ḍamina): Liable; the depositary becomes responsible for the value of the deposit if lost due to his violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A depositary is liable for loss only if he traveled despite the owner's prohibition or a dangerous route, or if he violated a specification of the city for preservation.

Liability of Depositary When Entrusting Deposit to Others
Explains liability and exceptions for depositary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary becomes liable for entrusting a deposit to others, and identify the exceptions that remove liability.

Core Idea
Entrusting a deposit to non-household triggers liability, except for trusted agents and emergencies.

A depositary (mudi‘) is entrusted with someone else's property. What happens if he gives it to another person? The general rule is that he becomes liable for loss if the deposit is lost in the hands of someone not in his household. But there are important exceptions: if he gives it to his own agent or trustee, or if an emergency (fire, flood) forces him to move it, he is not liable. This lesson unpacks those exceptions and the evidentiary requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The passage states: if the depositary preserves the deposit with someone other than his household and it is lost, he is liable. This is because the owner consented only to the depositary's custody.
Household (ʿiyāl) means family and dependents for whom the depositary is responsible.
2
Note the exception for trusted agents
If the depositary gives the deposit to his own agent (wakīl) or a trustee (amīn) whom he trusts with his own property—even if not in his household—he is not liable. This is a narration from Imam Muhammad and is the basis for fatwa.
The rationale: he treats the deposit like his own property; nothing more is required.
3
Recognize the emergency exception
If the depositary fears fire or drowning, he may transfer the deposit to a neighbor or another ship without liability, because necessity permits it implicitly.
The depositary must prove the emergency with evidence (bayyinah); a mere claim is not enough.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he preserves it with others, he is liable unless he fears. He (the commentator) said: 'And if he preserves it with others, he is liable' means even if the depositary preserves the deposit with someone not in his household and it is lost, the depositary is liable, because its owner did not consent to another's hand, and hands differ in trustworthiness. Do you not see that some hands may be trusted by the depositary but not by the owner, and it may be the opposite. And we have mentioned that if the depositary gives the deposit to his agent who is not in his household, or gives it to a trustee from his trustees whom he trusts with his property and who is not in his household, he does not guarantee, because he preserved it like he preserves his own property and made him like himself; nothing more is required of him. And this is a narration from Muhammad, and upon it is the fatwa, as in al-Nihayah and others, so remember. He said: 'Unless he fears fire' means unless the depositary fears fire such that a fire occurs in his house—may Allah protect us from that—so he fears the destruction of the deposit, or fears drowning likewise, so he gives the deposit to his neighbor in the case of fire, or to another ship in the case of drowning, and that deposit is lost, he does not guarantee, because he cannot preserve it in that situation except by this means, so he is implicitly permitted. He said in al-Hidayah: And he is not believed regarding that except with evidence, because he claims a necessity that drops liability.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when the depositary (al-mudi‘) entrusts the deposited item to someone else. The base ruling states that if he preserves the deposit with someone other than his own household (i.e., not his family or dependents), and it is lost, he is liable (damin). The reason is that the owner only consented to the depositary's own custody, and different hands have varying levels of trustworthiness. However, an exception is made if the depositary gives the deposit to his agent or a trusted trustee who is not in his household—in that case, he is not liable, because he treats the deposit like his own property. This is a narration from Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani, and it is the basis for fatwa. Additionally, if there is an emergency such as fire or flooding (drowning), the depositary may transfer the deposit to a neighbor or another ship without liability, because necessity permits it implicitly. However, the depositary must provide evidence (bayyinah) to prove the emergency; he is not believed on his mere claim. This ruling is from al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
عيال (‘iyāl): Household members; family and dependents of the depositary.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence; testimony or proof that the emergency (fire/drowning) actually occurred.
وكيل (wakīl): Agent; someone appointed by the depositary to act on his behalf.
أمين (amīn): Trustee; someone the depositary trusts with his own property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A depositary is liable for entrusting a deposit to a non-household member, but not if given to a trusted agent or in a genuine emergency, provided the emergency is proven by evidence.

Depositary Liability for Transferring the Deposit
Ruling and exception for depositary transferring deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of depositary liability when transferring a deposit, identify the exception of necessity, and apply the mufta bihi position (Imam Muhammad's narration) to determine liability.

Core Idea
A depositary who transfers a deposit to another is liable unless necessity compels, or he entrusts it to a trustee he trusts for his own wealth.

This passage from the commentary on al-Hidayah examines a key principle of wadi'ah: the depositary's liability when he entrusts the deposit to someone else. The general rule is that by doing so he becomes liable because the owner only consented to his hand. However, an exception exists for genuine necessity like fire or drowning. Additionally, Imam Muhammad's narration holds that if the depositary gives it to a person he trusts for his own wealth, he is not liable, and this is the operative fatwa. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying the law of deposits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule and its rationale
State the ruling: the depositary is liable if he entrusts the deposit to another without necessity. The reason is that the owner's consent is specific to the depositary's hand, and hands differ in trustworthiness.
The text says: 'لأن صاحبها لم يرض بيد غيره، والأيدي تختلف في الأمانة' — because the owner did not consent to the hand of another, and hands differ in trustworthiness.
2
Understand the exception for necessity
When the depositary fears fire or drowning and cannot preserve the deposit himself, he may give it to a neighbor or another ship. He is not liable because necessity implies implicit permission.
The exception is conditional on the inability to give it to his own household at that moment, as al-Samani adds.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi disagreement
Imam Muhammad narrates that if the depositary gives the deposit to a trustee he trusts for his own wealth, he is not liable, even without necessity. This is a valid ikhtilaf within the school.
The text states: 'وهذا رواية عن محمد، وعليه الفتوى' — this is a narration from Muhammad, and the fatwa is based on it.
4
Apply the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Imam Muhammad's narration. Therefore, a depositary is not liable if he entrusts the deposit to a person he trusts for his own wealth, provided that person is not a stranger but someone like his agent whom he trusts with his own property.
The text says: 'وهذا رواية عن محمد، وعليه الفتوى، كما في النهاية' — and the fatwa is upon it, as in al-Nihayah.

Then if he withheld it after the owner's demand for liability after the cause was established, so it becomes like when he claims permission to deposit. End. And he said in al-Muntaqa: This is if it is not known that a fire occurred in the depositary's house. If it is known, his statement is accepted without cause. End. So it is possible to interpret what is in al-Hidayah as when the occurrence of fire in his house is not known, and thereby reconciliation is achieved. And al-Samani said in Kamal al-Dirayah: They said: He is not liable only if he could not give it at that time to one of his household; if he could have given it and gave it to a stranger, he is liable because there is no necessity for him in that. Similarly, if he threw it into another ship and it perished before it was submerged, because its destruction occurred by his action. End. And similarly in al-Tabyin and others, and you have known the case of giving to a trustworthy stranger, so reflect. 1] He said: fire and drowning; al-haraq (fire) with two fatha is a noun from the burning of fire, and it is said for the fire itself. Thus in al-Misbah. And al-gharaq (drowning) is a verbal noun; it is said: the thing drowned in water, gharaqan, so it is ghariq, from the category of ta'iba, and also comes ghariq. Thus in it. And like the fear of drowning and fire is the fear of thieves. Stated by al-Tahawi and others. 2) He said: or in a ship; al-fulk like qofl is a ship, being singular when masculine and plural when feminine. Thus in al-Misbah.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling from the matn of al-Hidayah. The ruling states: If the depositary keeps the deposit (i.e., does not hand it to another), he is not liable if it is lost without his negligence; but if he entrusts it to someone other than those in his household, he becomes liable. The exception: unless he fears fire or drowning and is compelled to give it to a neighbor or another ship to save it. The commentary first explains the reason for liability: because the owner only consented to the depositary's hand, not another's. Different hands vary in trustworthiness. However, a narration from Imam Muhammad states that if the depositary gives it to a trustee he trusts for his own wealth, he is not liable, and this is the relied-upon fatwa. Then the exception is discussed: necessity implies implicit permission. The depositary must prove the necessity with evidence; otherwise, he is not believed. Al-Muntaqa adds that if the fire is known publicly, no proof is needed. Al-Samani further restricts the exception: it only applies if he could not give it to his own household at that moment. If he could, giving to a stranger makes him liable. Similarly, if he throws the deposit into another ship and it sinks before being submerged, he is liable because he caused the loss. The commentary ends with lexical notes on 'haraq' and 'fulk'.

Key Terms
وديعة (wadi'ah): A deposit: property entrusted to someone for safekeeping without compensation.
مودع (mudi' (depositor) or mudda' (depositary)?): The text uses 'مودع' for the depositary (the one who receives the deposit).
ضمن (damana): Became liable for compensation. If the deposit is lost, the depositary must pay its value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a depositary excused from liability if he gives the deposit to another person and it is lost?
A) When he gives it to a family member.
B) When he fears fire or drowning and cannot save it otherwise.
C) When he gives it to any trustworthy person.
D) When he gives it to a neighbor without any condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds a depositary liable for transferring a deposit to another unless compelled by necessity, or, per the mufta bihi, if the recipient is someone the depositary trusts for his own wealth.

Depositary Becomes Usurper by Withholding After Demand
Ruling: depositary becomes usurper by withholding deposit after owner's demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a depositary becomes a usurper (ghasib) if he withholds the deposit after the owner's demand while able to deliver, and identify the conditions triggering this transformation.

Core Idea
Withholding a deposit after demand while able to deliver turns a trustee into a usurper with strict liability.

A deposit (wadi‘ah) is initially an amanah (trust), meaning the depositary is only liable if negligent. However, once the owner demands its return and the depositary is able but refuses, the nature of possession changes. This lesson examines the precise conditions that transform a trusted holder into a wrongful usurper (ghasib), subjecting him to strict liability for any loss or damage.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the initial status
Start by understanding that a deposit is an amanah: the depositary is not liable for loss unless he commits negligence or transgression.
2
Identify the trigger: owner's demand
The owner (or his legal representative) must demand the return of the deposit. The demand can be explicit or constructive (e.g., through an agent). This demand disrupts the amanah status.
The text mentions 'even legally like an agent' – so demand through a proxy suffices.
3
Check the ability to deliver
At the time of demand, the depositary must be physically and legally able to deliver the deposit. If he cannot (e.g., item lost or inaccessible through no fault), it is not usurpation.
Ability to deliver is a key condition (shart).
4
Determine the consequence
If both demand and ability exist, but the depositary withholds, he becomes a ghasib (usurper). From that moment onwards, he is strictly liable – even if the property perishes without any fault on his part.
The liability (damān) is the same as that of a usurper: full compensation of the value.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٣) قوله : فإن حبسها...الخ ; يعني إن طلب الوديعة مالكها، ولو حكماً كالوكيل على ما في «المضمرات»، فحبسها المودعُ وأمسكها ، والحال أنه قادر على تسليمها ، فالمودع يصير غاصباً ويضمن.
3) He said: 'Then if he withholds it...etc.'; meaning if the owner of the deposit demands it, even legally like an agent as in al-Mudmarat, and the depositary withholds it and holds it back while he is able to deliver it, then the depositary becomes a usurper and is liable.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a brief commentary on the phrase 'فإن حبسها' from the earlier quote of al-Hidayah. It states a separate ruling: If the owner (or his agent) demands the deposit back, and the depositary is able to deliver it but withholds it, then the depositary becomes a usurper (ghasib) and is liable for any loss or damage. The key condition is the ability to deliver. The demand can be explicit or constructive (e.g., by an agent). This ruling emphasizes that the deposit remains a trust only as long as the depositary does not wrongfully detain it.

Key Terms
غاصب (ghasib): A usurper: one who wrongfully takes and holds property without right.
ضمن (damana): Liable; in this context, the depositary becomes liable for the value of the deposit from the moment of withholding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, when does a depositary become liable as a usurper (ghasib)?
A) If he uses the deposit without permission.
B) If he withholds it after the owner's demand while able to deliver.
C) If he loses it by negligence.
D) If he gives it to a family member without permission.

Key Takeaway
A depositary becomes a usurper and strictly liable from the moment he withholds the deposit after the owner's demand while being able to deliver.

Liability of Depositary for Detention and Denial
Explaining conditions of depositary's liability after request and denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a depositary becomes liable for detaining or denying a deposit, and apply the Hanafi distinction between movable and real estate.

Core Idea
Denial of a deposit makes the depositary liable even if he later confesses—but not for real estate according to the two shaykhs.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya delves into the precise moments when a depositary's liability attaches. It explores scenarios after the owner's request, distinguishing between detention (habs) and denial (jahd). Understanding these nuances is critical for the Hanafi judge and mufti when adjudicating trust disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what action triggers liability: detention after request when able to deliver, or denial of the deposit. The hukm is that the depositary becomes a guarantor (damin).
The default is that the depositary is an amin (trustee) and not liable for loss.
2
Locate the dalil
The author uses qiyas: the depositary's refusal to return after request is analogous to his falsely claiming permission to deposit elsewhere—both are transgressions (ta'addi) that nullify the trust.
No direct Quranic or hadith proof is cited; the reasoning is analogical.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illa) is the unjust refusal to return upon request, which transforms the depositary from an amin to a damin. This applies whether the refusal is explicit (detention) or implicit (denial).
Denial (jahd) is considered a form of ta'addi.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For real estate denial, Abu Hanifa has two narrations (liable in one, not in the other). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani both hold that denial of real estate does not impose liability.
The text in the source says 'contrary to Muhammad' but it is corrected to Abu Hanifa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): no liability for denial of real estate. This is supported by al-Tabyin.
The dispute does not extend to movable property; there is consensus on liability for movables.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة تعريفها وأحكامها. الحرق أو الغرق فوضعها عند جاره، أو في فلك آخر. فإن حبسها بعد طلب ربها للضمان بعد تحقق السبب، فصار كما إذا ادّعى الإذن في الإيداع. انتهى. وقال في «المنتقى»: هذا إذا لم يعلم أنه وقع في بيت المودع إحراق، فإن علم قبل قوله بلا سبب. انتهى. فيمكن حمل ما في «الهداية» على ما إذا لم يعلم بوقوع الحريق في بيته، وبه تحصيل التوفيق. وقال السمني في «كمال الدراية»: قالوا: إنّما لا يضمن إذا لم يمكنه أن يدفعها ذلك الوقت إلى من في عياله، حتى لو أمكنه فدفعها إلى أجنبي ضمن؛ لأنه لا ضرورة له فيه، وكذا لو ألقاها في سفينة أخرى فهلكت قبل أن تستغرقها؛ لأنّ هلاكها حصل بفعله. انتهى، وهكذا في «التبيين»، وغيره، وقد عرفت حال الدفع إلى الأجنبي الأمين فتفكر. [ثم قال الماتن: فإن حبسها بعد طلب ربها] قوله: قادرا على التسليم، أو جحدها معه، ثم أقر بها أو لا: أي جحدها مع طلب رب الوديعة يضمن سواء أقر بها بعد الجحود أو لا، وإنما قال: مع رب الوديعة وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لو استردها فقال: لم أقدر أن أحضر هذه الساعة، فتركها فهلكت لم يضمن؛ لأنه بالترك صار مودعا ابتداءً، وإلى أنه لو استردها فقال: اطلبها غدا، فلما كان من الغد قال: هلكت لم يضمن إن هلكت قبل قوله: اطلبها. وإلى أنه لو قال في السرّ: من أخبرك بعلامة كذا فادفعها إليه، ثم جاء لرجل بتلك العلامة ولم يدفعها إليه حتى هلكت لم يضمن، وإلى أنه لو طلب في أيام الفتنة، فقال: لم أقدر عليه هذه الساعة لبعدها أو لضيق الوقت فأغاروا على تلك الناحية، فقال: أغير عليها لم يضمن، والقول له في الكل، ذكره القهستاني نقلا عن «المحيط». وإلى أنه لو قال: احمل لي وديعتي اليوم، فقال: افعل، ولم يفعل حتى هلكت لا يضمن؛ لأن رد الوديعة ليس عليه، فلم يكن جالسا، ذكره قاضي خان في «فتاواه»، وإلى أن رسول المودع إذا طلب الوديعة ولم يدفعها المودع حتى هلكت لا يضمن. وقال نجم الدين النسفي: إنّه يضمن. وهذا القول لم يقبلوه؛ لأن المودع إذا صدق من ادعى أنه وكيل بقبض الوديعة لا يؤمر بدفع الوديعة إليه، لكن فيه أن الرسول ينطق على لسان المرسل، بخلاف الوكيل. كما صرحوا به فافهم. [قوله: قادرا على التسليم؛] وإنما قال ذلك؛ لأنه لو لم يقدر على تسليمها وحبسها بعد الطلب لا يضمن. [قوله: أو جحدها معه...الخ؛] يعني أو طلب الوديعة مالكها وجحدها المودع عند مالكها، سواء أخبر بها بعد الجحود أو لا، وإطلاق جحودها إشارة إلى أنه يضمن بجحود العقار أيضا بلا خلاف، وقال الإمام الحلواني: في ضمان العقار بالجحود عن أبي حنيفة روايتان. كما في شرح البرجندي نقلا عن «الخلاصة». وصرح في «التبيين» بأن الوديعة لو كانت عقارا لا يضمن بالجحود عند الشيخين خلافا لمحمد، والله أعلم.
Book of Deposit: Its Definition and Rulings. Fire or flood: so he places it with his neighbor, or in another ship. Then if he detains it after its owner's request, (he is liable) for liability after the cause has materialized, so it becomes as if he claimed permission to deposit. End. And he said in al-Muntaqa: This is if he does not know that a fire had occurred in the depositary's house; if he knows, his statement is not accepted without cause. End. So it is possible to interpret what is in al-Hidayah as the case when he does not know of the fire occurring in his house, and thereby reconciliation is achieved. And al-Samnani said in Kamal al-Dirayah: They said: He is not liable only if he could not at that time hand it over to someone in his household; if he could and handed it over to a stranger, he is liable because there is no necessity for him. Similarly, if he threw it into another ship and it perished before it had fully [loaded?], because its destruction occurred by his action. End. And likewise in al-Tabyin and others. And you have known the case of handing over to a trusted stranger, so reflect. [Then the author said: If he detains it after its owner's request.] His saying: 'Able to deliver, or denies it in his presence, then confesses to it or not': i.e., he denies it upon the request of the owner of the deposit — he is liable whether he confesses after denial or not. He only said 'in the presence of the owner of the deposit' and therein is an indication that if he reclaimed it and said, 'I could not bring it at this hour,' and left it and it perished, he is not liable because by leaving it he became a depositary anew; and an indication that if he reclaimed it and said, 'Ask for it tomorrow,' and when it was the next day he said, 'It perished,' he is not liable if it perished before he said 'Ask for it.' And an indication that if he said in secret, 'Whoever tells you such-and-such a sign, give it to him,' then a man came with that sign and he did not give it to him until it perished, he is not liable. And an indication that if he was asked during the days of turmoil and said, 'I could not get it this hour because of its distance or lack of time,' and they raided that area, and he said, 'It was raided,' he is not liable, and his word is accepted in all cases. Al-Quhistani mentioned it quoting from al-Muhit. And an indication that if he said, 'Carry my deposit for me today,' and he said, 'I will do it,' and he did not do it until it perished, he is not liable because returning the deposit is not obligatory upon him, so he was not sitting (i.e., refusing). Qadi Khan mentioned it in his Fatawa. And an indication that if the messenger of the depositary requested the deposit and the depositary did not hand it over until it perished, he is not liable. Najm al-Din al-Nasafi said that he is liable. And this statement was not accepted by them because the depositary, if he believes someone who claims to be an agent for receiving the deposit, is not ordered to hand over the deposit to him. But therein is that the messenger speaks on behalf of the sender, unlike the agent. As they stated, so understand. [His saying: 'Able to deliver;'] He only said that because if he was not able to deliver it and detained it after request, he is not liable. [His saying: 'Or denies it in his presence...etc.'] Means: the owner of the deposit requests it and the depositary denies it in the owner's presence, whether he later informs about it or not. And the absolute mention of denial indicates that he is liable by denial of real estate as well without disagreement. Imam al-Halwani said: Regarding the liability for real estate by denial, from Abu Hanifa there are two narrations, as in Sharh al-Barjandi quoting from al-Khulasah. And it is stated in al-Tabyin that if the deposit is real estate, he is not liable by denial according to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Muhammad? Actually, it says 'contrary to Muhammad' but it might be a mistake; it should be 'contrary to Abu Hanifa'. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter of deposits (al-Wadi'ah) from the matn of al-Wiqayah. It opens by discussing the ruling when a depositary, fearing fire or flood, moves the deposit to a neighbor or another ship. The base text states that if he then detains it after the owner's request, he becomes liable. The commentator explains that liability attaches because the cause (the request) has been realized, analogizing it to the case where the depositary falsely claims he had permission to deposit elsewhere. He then cites al-Muntaqa to distinguish between whether the depositary knew of a fire in his house or not; if he did not know, his action is excused. Al-Samnani adds that the depositary is only excused if he could not hand the deposit to a household member; if he could and gave it to a stranger, he is liable. Likewise, if he throws it into another ship and it sinks before being fully loaded, he is liable because his action caused the loss. The commentary then explains the phrase 'if he detains it after its owner's request' from the matn, clarifying that the depositary becomes liable (as a usurper) if he is able to deliver but refuses, or if he denies the deposit outright, even if he later confesses. Several scenarios illustrate when he is not liable: if he reclaims the deposit but says he cannot bring it now and leaves it (he becomes a new depositary), if he asks the owner to come tomorrow but the deposit perishes before the owner arrives, if he instructs to give it to someone with a sign and that person comes but the depositary does not hand it over until it perishes (no liability because the depositary did not refuse delivery), if during turmoil he says he cannot retrieve it due to distance and it is destroyed (his word is accepted), if the owner asks him to carry the deposit and he agrees but then fails and it perishes (no liability because returning is not obligatory on the depositary), and if a messenger requests the deposit and the depositary does not hand it over until it perishes (no liability, contrary to Nasafi's view, because the messenger is not like an agent—the depositary is not obligated to believe him). Finally, the passage discusses denial (juhud). The depositary is liable if he denies the deposit upon the owner's request, even if he later admits. This includes denial of real estate, though there is a difference of opinion: according to the two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), denial of real estate does not cause liability, while Muhammad (or Abu Hanifa) has two narrations. The relied-upon position is that of the two shaykhs.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadī'ah): A trust (amanah) placed with someone for safekeeping without compensation.
المودع (al-mūdi'): The person with whom the deposit is left (depositary).
الجحد (al-jahd): Denial of the deposit when the owner requests it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition does a depositary become liable if he moves the deposit to another ship due to fear of fire?
A) If he moves it to any other ship.
B) If he is unable to hand it to a household member and gives it to a stranger instead.
C) If he gives it to a stranger when he could have given it to a household member.
D) If the other ship sinks before the deposit is fully loaded.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a depositary who unjustly detains or denies a deposit upon the owner's request becomes a guarantor, except for real estate denial according to the two shaykhs.

Conditions for Liability of Depositary upon Denial of Deposit
Critical refinement of liability conditions for depositary's denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six conditions under which a depositary's denial creates liability and identify the Hanafi position on denial of real estate and denial before non-owners.

Core Idea
A depositary's denial does not automatically create liability; specific conditions must be met.

This lesson unpacks a critical refinement from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. The author argues that earlier matns were too absolute; the depositary's denial only triggers liability when bounded by precise conditions: before the owner, after demand, with moving of movable property, without fear, and without re-entrustment. Understanding this protects against unjust claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
Identify that the act is the depositary's denial (juhud) of receiving the deposit. Determine the default ruling: does denial automatically create liability (daman)?
The passage clarifies that liability is not automatic; it depends on conditions.
2
List the conditions for liability
From the text, extract the conditions that must be present for the denial to create liability: denial before owner, after demand, moving the deposit, deposit is movable, no fear of destruction, and no re-entrustment (new ibda').
These are derived from the phrase 'قيّدنا بكونه...'
3
Understand the exceptions
Identify that denial before a non-owner is considered preservation and does not create liability. Also, denial of real estate does not create liability according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
Zufar disagrees on all denials; Muhammad disagrees on real estate.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf exempt real estate; Muhammad holds liability. The commentator indicates the relied-upon position follows Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
Also, the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that denial before non-owner is not liable.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Conclude that the mufta bihi (operative legal opinion) is: no liability for denial of real estate; no liability for denial before non-owner; liability only when all other conditions are met.
This is consistent with the majority Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوديعة تعريفها وأحكامها
واعلم أن صاحب «المنح» قال في شرح التنوير : وبخلاف إقراره بعد جحوده بأن قال : لم تودعني عند مالكها بعد طلب ردّها ونقلها من مكانها وقت الإنكار، وكانت الوديعة منقولاً ، ولم يكن هنالك من يخاف منه عليها ؛ أي على الوديعة، ولم يحضرها ؛ أي الوديعة بعد الجحود لمالكها، فإنّه لا يزول الضمان ؛ لأنَّ الجحود رفع للعقد ينفسخ فيه العقد فلا يعود إلا بعقد جديد، كجحود الوكيل الوكالة، وجحود أحد المتبايعين البيع. قيدنا بكونه أنكر الإيداع ؛ لأنَّ المودَعَ لو ادعى أن المالك وهبها منه أو باعها، وأنكرها صاحبها ، ثمَّ هلكت لا ضمان على المودع. كما في «الخلاصة». وقيدنا بكون الإنكار عند المالك ؛ لأنَّ جحودها عند غيره لا يوجب الضمان. وقيّدنا بعد الطلب ؛ لأنه لو قال : ما حال وديعتي عندك، يشكر على حفظها فجحدها لا ضمان عليه. وقيدنا بكونه نقلها ؛ لأنه لو لم ينقلها من مكانها حال جحوده فهلكت لا ضمان عليه. كذا في «الخلاصة» معزياً إلى «الأجناس». وقيدنا بكونها منقولاً ؛ لأنها لو كانت عقاراً لا يضمن بالجحود عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف خلافاً لمحمد الله في الأصح ، ذكره الزَّيْلَعِيُّ من «كتاب الغصب». وقيّدنا بكونه لم يكن من يخاف عليها منه ؛ لأنه لو جحدها في وجه عدو يخافُ عليها التلف إن أقرَّ ثم هلكت لا يضمنها ؛ لأنه إنما أراد حفظها. وقيدنا بكونه لم يحضر ؛ لأنه لو جحدها فقال له صاحبها : دعها وديعة عندك فهلكت، فإن أمكنه أخذها فلم يأخذها لم يضمن ؛ لأنه إبداع جديد، وإن لم يمكن أخذها ضمن ؛ لأنه لم يتم الرد كذا في «الاختيار»
إذا علمت ذلك ظهر أن كلام «الكنز» ، و«الوقاية»، وغيرهما من أصحاب المتون في هذا المحل إطلاق في محل التقييد. انتهى.
لأنه إن جحدها مع غير المالك لا يضمن ؛ لأنَّ هذا من باب الحفظ ، وإن جَهل المودع الوديعة عند الموت يصير غاصبا
أقول وبالله التوفيق وبيده أزمة التحقيق : إنَّ التقييد بكون الإنكار عند المالك يفيده قول المصنف له : أو جحدها معه. وأظهره قولُ الشارح له : وإنما قال : مع رب المال... الخ. وأما التقييد بكون الإنكار بعد الطلب فلا حاجة إليه ؛ لأنَّ الإنكار لا يكونُ حقيقة إلا بعد الطلب، وما ذكره من قوله : لو قال له : ما حال وديعتي عندك ليشكر على حفظها، فجحدها فهذا ليس بجحودٍ حقيقية وكذا لم يعتبر وبني عليه حكم عدم الضمان. وأما التقييد بكون الوديعة منقولاً فلا حاجة إليه أيضاً ؛ لعدم تصوّر غـصـب العقار، فتقرر فيه عدم الضمان ، ولعل مذهب محمد الله كان قوياً في نظره، فأراد قوله : بالإطلاق، وقس عليه التقييد بكون نقلها. وأما التقييد بكونه لم يحضرها فلا حاجة إليه أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ المودع لو أحضر كان إبداعاً جديداً فيخرج بنفسه عما نحن فيه، فتفكر ولا تعجل. قوله : لا يضمن ؛ وقال زفر له : يضمن ؛ لأنَّ المودع صار غاصباً بالجحود، فالجحود سبب للضمان سواء كان عند المالك أو غيره كالإتلاف حقيقة. ولنا : إنَّ الجحود عند غير المالك يعد من باب الحفظ ؛ لأنه يقطعُ طمع الطامعين عنها وبه قالت الثلاثة.
قوله : وإن جهل.... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ المودَعَ لو جهل الوديعة عند موته بحيث لا تعرفُ حالُها بأن مات ولم يبين حالها، فالمودَعُ يصيرُ غاصباً ويضمن، وتصير تلك الوديعة ديناً في تركته ، نعم إذا علم المودَعُ أنَّ وارثه يعلمها ولم يبينها فلا ضمان عليه. قوله : يصير غاصباً ؛ ويضمن كما في سائر الأمانات إلا في ناظر أودع غلات الوقف ثم مات مُجْهِلاً فإنّه لا يضمن، وفي قاض ماتَ مُجْهِلا لأموال اليتامى ، وفي سلطان أودع بعض الغنيمة عند غاز ثم مات مُجهلاً ، وفي أحد المتفاوضين إذا مات ولم يبين حال المال الذي في يديه، فإنه لا يضمن نصيب شريكه.
Book of Deposit: Its Definition and Rulings. Know that the author of 'al-Minhah' said in 'Sharh al-Tanwir': Contrary to his acknowledgment after his denial, such that he said: 'You did not entrust me with it' in the presence of its owner after demand for its return and moving it from its place at the time of denial, and the deposit was movable, and there was no one to be feared for it, i.e., for the deposit, and he did not produce it, i.e., the deposit after denial to its owner, then liability does not cease because denial nullifies the contract, the contract is dissolved thereby and does not return except with a new contract, like the agent's denial of agency, and one of the two parties to a sale denying the sale. We restricted it to his denial of the entrustment because if the depositary claimed that the owner gifted it to him or sold it, and the owner denied it, then it perished, there is no liability on the depositary. As in 'al-Khulasa'. And we restricted it to the denial being in the presence of the owner because denial before others does not necessitate liability. And we restricted it to being after demand because if he said: 'What is the condition of my deposit with you?' thanking him for preserving it, and he denied it, there is no liability on him. And we restricted it to his moving it because if he did not move it from its place at the time of his denial and it perished, there is no liability on him. Likewise in 'al-Khulasa' attributed to 'al-Ajnas'. And we restricted it to it being movable because if it were real estate, liability would not be incurred by denial according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, contrary to Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, in the most correct opinion, mentioned by al-Zayla'i from 'Kitab al-Ghasb'. And we restricted it to there not being anyone to be feared for it because if he denied it in the face of an enemy who fears its destruction if he acknowledges, then it perishes, he does not guarantee it because he only intended to preserve it. And we restricted it to his not having produced it because if he denied it and its owner said to him: 'Leave it as a deposit with you,' and it perished, then if he could take it and did not, he does not guarantee because it is a new entrustment; and if he could not take it, he guarantees because the return was not completed. Likewise in 'al-Ikhtiyar'. If you know this, it becomes clear that the statements of 'al-Kanz', 'al-Wiqayah', and other authors of matns in this regard are absolute in a place requiring restriction. End. Because if he denies it with someone other than the owner, he does not guarantee because this is of the category of preservation; and if the depositary is ignorant of the deposit at the time of death, he becomes a usurper. I say, with Allah is success and in His hand are the reins of verification: The restriction of denial being in the presence of the owner is indicated by the author's statement: 'or he denies it with him.' And it is made apparent by the commentator's statement: 'He only said: with the owner...' etc. As for the restriction of denial being after demand, there is no need for it because denial is not literally realized except after demand, and what he mentioned of his statement: 'If he said: What is the condition of my deposit with you?' to thank him for preserving it, and he denied it, this is not real denial and thus not considered, and the ruling of non-liability is built upon it. As for the restriction of the deposit being movable, there is also no need for it because usurpation of real estate is inconceivable, so non-liability is established; perhaps the school of Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him, was strong in his view, so he intended his statement absolutely; and analogize to it the restriction of moving it. As for the restriction of him not producing it, there is also no need for it because if the depositary produces it, it is a new entrustment and thus exits from what we are discussing; so ponder and do not hasten. He said: 'He does not guarantee'; and Zufar said: He guarantees because the depositary becomes a usurper by denial, so denial is a cause for guarantee whether before the owner or others, like actual destruction. And for us: Denial before other than the owner is considered from the category of preservation because it cuts off the greed of the greedy, and the three said this. He said: 'And if he is ignorant...' meaning if the depositary is ignorant of the deposit at his death such that its condition is not known, that he dies without clarifying its condition, then the depositary becomes a usurper and guarantees, and that deposit becomes a debt in his estate. Yes, if the depositary knows that his heir knows of it and does not clarify, there is no liability on him. He said: 'He becomes a usurper'; and he guarantees as in all other trusts except in the case of a supervisor who entrusts proceeds of a waqf and then dies without clarifying, for he does not guarantee; and in the case of a judge who dies without clarifying the property of orphans; and in the case of a sultan who entrusts part of the spoils to a warrior and then dies without clarifying; and in the case of one of two partners in a partnership (mufawada) if he dies without clarifying the condition of the wealth in his hands, then he does not guarantee the share of his partner.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a critical refinement (hashiya) of the matn and sharh on the rulings of deposit (wadi'ah). The central issue is whether a depositary's denial (juhud) of the deposit eliminates liability (daman) or not. The text first lists conditions under which denial does NOT remove liability (i.e., liability persists). These conditions are drawn from earlier sources: the denial must be to the owner, after demand, with moving of the deposit, the deposit movable, no fear of destruction, and the deposit not produced. If these conditions are met, the original liability remains because denial dissolves the contract of deposit and only a new contract can restore the trust. The text then notes that the statements of al-Kanz and al-Wiqayah are absolute and need these restrictions. The commentator (probably the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah) then analyzes each restriction, arguing that some are unnecessary because they follow from the nature of the contract (e.g., denial after demand is implicit in the concept of denial). He also addresses the intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that denial of real estate does not create liability, while Muhammad holds it does, but the correct position (al-ashah) is with Abu Hanifa. Zufar holds that denial always creates liability, but the three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold that denial before a non-owner is considered preservation and thus no liability. Finally, the passage discusses the case of a depositary dying without clarifying the deposit: he becomes a usurper and the deposit is a debt in his estate, except for certain public trustees (waqf supervisor, judge, sultan, partner in mufawada) who are exempt due to their public role.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial by the depositary that he received the deposit, often implying a breach of trust.
وديعة (wadi'ah): A contract of deposit where one party entrusts property to another for safekeeping without compensation.
ضمان (daman): Liability for the value of the deposit if it is lost or damaged due to the depositary's wrongful act.
مستودع (مودع) (mustawda' / mawda'): The person who receives the deposit (depositary).
إبداع (ibda'): The act of entrusting a deposit; creating a new contract of deposit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following conditions is NOT required for the depositary's denial to create liability?
A) The denial is before the owner.
B) The denial is after demand for return.
C) The deposit is real estate.
D) The depositary moves the deposit during denial.

Key Takeaway
A depositary's denial only creates liability if it occurs before the owner after demand, with moving of a movable deposit, without fear, and without subsequent re-entrustment.

When Does Denial of a Deposit Incur Liability? The Hanafi Position
Explains conditions for guarantee when depositary denies the deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why denial of a deposit before a non-owner does not incur guarantee according to the Hanafi school, and contrast this with Imam Zufar's view.

Core Idea
Denial of a deposit before a non-owner is preservation, not usurpation, and does not trigger liability.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about the effect of a depositary's denial (juhud) in a wadi'ah contract. While common sense might suggest that any denial makes the depositary a guarantor, the leading Hanafi imams distinguished based on the audience of the denial. Understanding this distinction reveals a deeper principle: actions are judged by their consequences, and denial before a non-owner can actually protect the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The depositary guarantees (daman) only if he denies the deposit before the owner. If he denies before a non-owner, he does not guarantee.
This is the position of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the three imams).
2
Follow the Hanafi reasoning
Denial before a non-owner is considered preservation (hifz) because it cuts off the greed of claimants and protects the deposit.
The act is classified as intentional preservation, not transgression (ta'addi).
3
Analyze Imam Zufar's opposing argument
Zufar holds that any denial causes guarantee, analogizing it to actual destruction (itlaf). He sees denial itself as usurpation (ghasb) regardless of audience.
Zufar's view is a minority position within the Hanafi school.
4
Compare the two positions and identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under the majority view, liability arises only when denial is to the owner; under Zufar's view, it arises in all cases.
The majority view is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa).
5
Examine the unnecessary restrictions discussed
The commentator explains three restrictions often mentioned but not needed: (1) denial must be after demand (inherent), (2) deposit must be movable (ghasb of immovable is impossible), (3) depositary must not have brought the deposit (that would be a new deposit).
These restrictions refine the ruling but do not change it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنه إن جحدها مع غير المالك لا يضمن؛ لأن هذا من باب الحفظ، وإن جَهل المودع الوديعة عند الموت يصير غاصبًا. أقول وبالله التوفيق وبيده أزمة التحقيق: إن التقييد بكون الإنكار عند المالك يفيده قول المصنف له: أو جحدها معه. وأظهره قولُ الشارح له: وإنما قال: مع رب المال... الخ. وأما التقييد بكون الإنكار بعد الطلب فلا حاجة إليه؛ لأن الإنكار لا يكونُ حقيقة إلا بعد الطلب، وما ذكره من قوله: لو قال له: ما حال وديعتي عندك ليشكر على حفظها، فجحدها فهذا ليس بجحودٍ حقيقية وكذا لم يعتبر وبني عليه حكم عدم الضمان. وأما التقييد بكون الوديعة منقولاً فلا حاجة إليه أيضاً؛ لعدم تصوّر غصب العقار، فتقرر فيه عدم الضمان، ولعل مذهب محمد الله كان قوياً في نظره، فأراد قوله: بالإطلاق، وقس عليه التقييد بكون نقلها. وأما التقييد بكونه لم يحضرها فلا حاجة إليه أيضاً؛ لأن المودع لو أحضر كان إبداعاً جديداً فيخرج بنفسه عما نحن فيه، فتفكر ولا تعجل. قوله: لا يضمن؛ وقال زفر له: يضمن؛ لأن المودع صار غاصباً بالجحود، فالجحود سبب للضمان سواء كان عند المالك أو غيره كالإتلاف حقيقة. ولنا: إن الجحود عند غير المالك يعد من باب الحفظ؛ لأنه يقطعُ طمع الطامعين عنها وبه قالت الثلاثة.
Because if he denies it [the deposit] with someone other than the owner, he does not guarantee; because this is from the category of preservation. And if the depositary makes the deposit unknown at death, he becomes a usurper. I say: and with Allah is success and in His hand is the control of verification: Indeed, the restriction that denial is with the owner is indicated by the author's statement: 'or he denies it with him.' And its clarification is in the commentator's statement: 'He only said: with the owner...' etc. As for the restriction that denial is after demand, there is no need for it; because denial does not actually occur except after demand. And what he mentioned from his statement: 'If someone says: what is the status of my deposit with you so that he may be thanked for preserving it, and he denies it' – this is not actual denial, and similarly it was not considered, and the ruling of no guarantee was built upon it. As for the restriction that the deposit is movable property, there is also no need for it; because usurpation of immovable property is inconceivable, so it is established that there is no guarantee. Perhaps the madhhab of Muhammad was strong in his view, so he intended his statement in absolute terms, and analogize to it the restriction of its transfer. As for the restriction that he did not bring it, there is also no need for it; because if the depositary brought it, it would become a new entrustment and would itself exit from what we are discussing. So ponder and do not rush. His statement: 'He does not guarantee' – and Zufar said against him: 'He guarantees; because the depositary became a usurper by denial, so denial is a cause for guarantee whether it is with the owner or others, like actual destruction.' And for us: Denial with other than the owner is considered from the category of preservation because it cuts off the greed of the greedy, and the three [imams] said this.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of denial in a deposit contract. The core ruling is that if the depositary (trustee) denies the deposit in front of the owner, he becomes a guarantor (daman). However, if he denies it in front of a non-owner, he does not guarantee because such denial is considered a form of preservation (hifz) – it deters potential thieves or claimants. The commentator refines this by explaining that several restrictions (taqyidat) commonly mentioned are not actually needed: denial is only real when the owner demands; the deposit must be movable (since ghasb of immovable is not possible); and the depositary cannot have already brought the deposit forward (which would constitute a new deposit). The key dispute is with Imam Zufar (d. 158/775), a Hanafi scholar, who held that denial itself causes guarantee regardless of audience, analogizing it to destruction (itlaf). The Hanafi response, upheld by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad (the 'three'), is that denial before a non-owner is preservation, not usurpation, because it protects the property from covetous parties. Thus the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that no guarantee arises from denial outside the owner's presence.

Key Terms
جحود (juhud): Denial or disavowal of the deposit by the depositary.
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or destruction of the deposit.
حفظ (hifz): Preservation; an action intended to protect the property, which does not incur liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a depositary denies the deposit in front of a third party (not the owner), what is the ruling?
A) He becomes a guarantor because denial is akin to destruction.
B) He does not guarantee because such denial is considered preservation.
C) He becomes a guarantor only if the third party demands the deposit.
D) He is excused because he was protecting the deposit.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, denial of a deposit only triggers liability when made to the owner; denial to a non-owner is considered an act of preservation.

Death of Depositary Without Clarifying Deposit: General Rule and Exceptions
Ruling on liability for death without clarification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of liability when a depositary dies without clarifying the deposit and list the ten excepted cases where no liability arises.

Core Idea
Death without clarification makes the depositary liable as a usurper, but ten specific cases exempt from liability.

This passage presents a key ruling in Hanafi fiqh concerning trusts (amanat): if a depositary dies without leaving information about the deposit, he is legally treated as a usurper and his estate must compensate the owner. However, the school recognizes important exceptions—ten specific situations where no liability attaches, even if the depositary dies without clarifying. These exceptions are based on the nature of the relationship or the circumstances of the trust.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
Identify the baseline ruling: the depositary who dies without clarifying the deposit becomes a ghasib (usurper) and must guarantee its value; it becomes a debt against his estate.
This applies to all trusts (amanat) unless an exception applies.
2
Recognize the exception when the heir knows
If the depositary knows that his heir already knows about the deposit and its details, no liability arises. This is because the trust can still be fulfilled.
This is a logical exception: the purpose of clarification is served.
3
List the ten excepted cases
Memorize the ten cases listed in the text where the depositary is not liable despite dying without clarification. These include: superintendent (nazir) of waqf, judge for orphans' funds, sultan for spoils, mutual partner (mufawada) for partner's share, executor (wasi), father for son's property, heir for what was deposited with testator, person for wind-blown items, person for property placed without knowledge, and a minor under interdiction (mahjur).
The list is from al-Ashbah; later scholars like al-Shurunbulali expanded it to nineteen.
4
Categorize the exceptions
Group the exceptions by rationale: public officials (nazir, judge, sultan) acting in public interest; family relations (father, heir, minor) where knowledge is presumed; involuntary possession (wind-blown, placed without knowledge); and special partnerships (mufawada).
The common thread: the depositary is not considered negligent or transgressive in these roles.
5
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether liability applies by checking if the depositary's role falls within the exceptions. If not, the general rule of guarantee applies.
Practice with case studies to internalize the boundaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وإن جهل.... الخ؛ يعني إن المودَعَ لو جهل الوديعة عند موته بحيث لا تعرف حالها بأن مات ولم يبين حالها، فالمودَعُ يصير غاصباً ويضمن، وتصير تلك الوديعة ديناً في تركته، نعم إذا علم المودَعُ أن وارثه يعلمها ولم يبينها فلا ضمان عليه. قوله: يصير غاصباً؛ ويضمن كما في سائر الأمانات إلا في ناظر أودع غلات الوقف ثم مات مُجْهِلاً فإنّه لا يضمن، وفي قاض ماتَ مُجْهِلا لأموال اليتامى، وفي سلطان أودع بعض الغنيمة عند غاز ثم مات مُجهلاً، وفي أحد المتفاوضين إذا مات ولم يبين حال المال الذي في يديه، فإنه لا يضمن نصيب شريكه. وفي وصي إذا مات مُجْهِلا فلا ضمان عليه، وفي أب إذا مات مجهلاً مال ابنه، وفي وراث إذا مات مُجهلاً ما أودع عند مورثه، وفي رجل ماتَ مُجْهِلا لما ألقته الريح في بيته، وفي رجل مات مُجهلاً لما وضعه مالكه في بيته بغير علمه، وفي صبي مات مُجهلاً لما أودع عنده محجوراً، فهذه عشرة مسائل استثناها صاحب «الأشباه»، وغيره. وأما الشرنبلالي فعد المستثناة تسعة عشر مسئلة. ذكـرهـا صـاحب «الدر المختار»، وغيره نقلاً عن «شرح الوهبانية» للشرنبلالي.
His statement: 'And if he makes it unknown...' – meaning that if the depositary makes the deposit unknown at the time of his death such that its condition is not known, i.e., he died without clarifying its condition, then the depositary becomes a usurper and guarantees, and that deposit becomes a debt in his estate. Yes, if the depositary knows that his heir knows about it and he does not clarify it, then there is no guarantee upon him. His statement: 'He becomes a usurper' – and he guarantees as in all other trusts, except in: a superintendent (nazir) who deposited the revenues of a waqf and then died without clarifying – he does not guarantee; a judge who died without clarifying the property of orphans; a sultan who deposited part of the spoils with a warrior and then died without clarifying; one of two partners (mutafawidan) if he died without clarifying the state of the property in his hands – he does not guarantee his partner's share; an executor (wasi) if he died without clarifying – there is no guarantee upon him; a father if he died without clarifying his son's property; an heir if he died without clarifying what was deposited with his testator; a man who died without clarifying what the wind threw into his house; a man who died without clarifying what its owner placed in his house without his knowledge; a minor who died without clarifying what was deposited with him while he was under interdiction (mahjur). These are ten cases which the author of al-Ashbah and others excepted. As for al-Shurunbulali, he counted the excepted cases as nineteen. The author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar and others mentioned them, quoting from Sharh al-Wahbaniyyah of al-Shurunbulali.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage explains the general rule: if a depositary (trustee) dies without clarifying the details of a deposit (e.g., what it is, where it is, or to whom it belongs), he is treated as a usurper (ghasib) and his estate is liable to compensate the owner. The deposit becomes a debt against his estate. However, there is an exception: if the depositary knows that his heir already knows about the deposit and its details, then no liability arises because the heir can fulfill the trust. The passage then lists ten specific exemptions where even if the trustee dies without clarifying, he is not liable. These exceptions include: a waqf superintendent, a judge handling orphan funds, a sultan with spoils, a partner in a mutual partnership (mufawada), an executor (wasi), a father holding his son's property, an heir holding what was deposited with his testator, a person who finds something blown by wind into his house, a person whose property is placed in his house without his knowledge, and a minor who is under interdiction (mahjur). The reason for these exemptions is usually that the nature of their position or the circumstances imply a lack of transgression. The passage also notes that later scholars like al-Shurunbulali expanded the list to nineteen cases, but the basic principle remains: death without clarification generally triggers guarantee, except in specific established exceptions.

Key Terms
أمانات (amanat): Trusts; property held on trust (like deposit, loan for use, etc.).
مجهلاً (muhjilan): A state of dying without clarifying the deposit's details.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who takes property wrongfully. Here, the depositary is legally treated as a usurper due to his failure to clarify.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A father who died without clarifying his son's property.
B) A judge who died without clarifying orphans' property.
C) A partner in a mufawada partnership who died without clarifying partnership property.
D) A commercial agent (wakil) who died without clarifying goods entrusted to him.

Key Takeaway
A depositary who dies without clarifying the deposit is generally liable as a usurper, but the Hanafi school exempts ten specific roles based on their public function or special circumstances.
