Mixing Deposit with Own Property: When Liability Arises
Ruling on depositary mixing deposit with own property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mixing a deposit with the depositary's own property and identify the key condition that determines liability.

Core Idea
Mixing without permission only triggers guarantee if separation is difficult — easy separation leaves the owner's right intact.

This passage explains when a depositary who mixes the deposited item with his own property becomes a guarantor. The key is whether the mixture becomes indistinguishable with difficulty (kulfah). We will explore the general ruling, the exception for easy separation, and the istihsan-based rule for good and bad dirhams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The depositary mixes the deposit with his own property without the owner's permission. What is the default ruling?
If mixing is with permission, the depositary becomes a partner – not a guarantor.
2
Determine the condition for guarantee
Guarantee (daman) arises only when the mixture becomes 'indistinguishable' – meaning separation is only possible with difficulty (kulfah).
The criterion is difficulty, not absolute impossibility.
3
Distinguish easy separation
If the items can be separated easily (e.g., walnuts from almonds, white dirhams from black), the owner's right to his specific property is not cut off – no guarantee arises (by consensus).
4
Examine the istihsan exception
Mixing good dirhams with bad (counterfeit) dirhams – even though theoretically separable by assay – is treated as indistinguishable by istihsan, because such mixing is pervasive in circulation. This cuts off the owner's specific right.
This departs from strict qiyas (analogy) for practical convenience.
5
Identify who is liable
Only the depositary who actually mixes bears the guarantee. If a third party or a household member mixes, the depositary is not liable; the mixer bears the guarantee regardless of age.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو خلط بماله حتى لا يتميز. يعني أو خلط المودعُ الوديعة بماله بغير إذن من المالك؛ لأنه إن خلطها بإذنه كان شريكا فيها، أطلق في الخلط فشمل خلط الجنس بالجنس، أو بغير جنسه كخلط الزيت بالشيرج، والحنطة بالشعير وبالحنطة، والفضة بالفضة بعد الإذابة. قيد بكون المودع بيد الخالط لأن الخالط لو كان أجنبياً أو مَن في عياله لا يضمن المودع، والضمان على الخالط صغيراً كان أو كبيراً، ولا يضمن أبوه. كذا ذكره في «المنح» نقلاً عن «الخلاصة». قوله: حتى لا يتميز... الخ؛ يعني بحيث لا يتميّزُ المخلوط بالمخلوط به، وهذا إذا كانت العبارة بصيغة المذكّر كما في أكثر النسخ، وأما إذا كانت بصيغة المؤنَّثِ كما في بعض النسخ فالمعنى أنه بحيثُ لا تتميز تلك الوديعة المخلوطة بما خلطها به. والمراد أنها لا تتميز إلا بالكلفة، لا عدم إمكان التمييز مطلقاً، فلو كان الوصول إليها على وجه التيسير، كما إذا خلط الجوز باللوز، والدراهم السود والدراهم البيض، فلا ينقطع حق المالك إجماعاً. كما صرح به في «المنح»، وغيره. وقال في «المجتبى شرح مختصر القدوري : خلط الدراهم الجياد بالزيوف يقطع حق المالك استحساناً؛ لأن الجياد لا تخلو عن الزيوف، والزيوف عن الجياد، ولو صب.
Or he mixed with his property until it is indistinguishable. Meaning: or the depositary mixed the deposit with his property without permission from the owner; because if he mixed it with his permission, he would be a partner in it. He made it absolute regarding mixing, so it includes mixing same genus with same genus, or with a different genus like mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley and with wheat, silver with silver after melting. It is restricted that the depositary is the one who mixes, because if the mixer was a stranger or someone in his household, the depositary does not guarantee, and the guarantee is on the mixer whether young or old, and his father does not guarantee. This is mentioned in al-Minah quoting from al-Khulasa. His statement: 'until it is indistinguishable' – meaning such that the mixed thing cannot be distinguished from what it is mixed with. This is if the expression is in the masculine form as in most copies. But if it is in the feminine form as in some copies, the meaning is that that mixed deposit cannot be distinguished from what it was mixed with. What is meant is that it cannot be distinguished except with difficulty, not the absolute impossibility of distinction. For example, if one mixes walnuts with almonds, or black dirhams with white dirhams, then the owner's right is not cut off by consensus, as explicitly stated in al-Minah and others. And it is said in al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: mixing good dirhams with bad dirhams cuts off the owner's right by istihsan, because good dirhams are not free of bad ones, and bad ones of good ones.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling on a depositary mixing the deposit with his own property. The general principle: if the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property without the owner's permission and in such a way that the two become indistinguishable (i.e., cannot be separated without difficulty), then the depositary becomes a guarantor because he has effectively prevented the owner from reclaiming his specific property. However, if the mixing is done with the owner's permission, the depositary becomes a partner (sharik) in the mixed property, with shared ownership. The ruling is unrestricted: it includes mixing same genus (e.g., wheat with wheat) or different genus (e.g., oil with sesame oil, silver with silver after melting). Importantly, the mixer must be the depositary himself; if a third party (ajnabi) or someone in the depositary's household does the mixing without his involvement, the depositary is not liable; instead, the mixer bears the guarantee, regardless of age (even a child), and the father does not guarantee for the child. The key condition for guarantee is 'until it is indistinguishable' – meaning that separation is only possible with difficulty (kulfah). If separation is easy (like walnuts from almonds, or white dirhams from black), then the owner's right to his specific property is not cut off by consensus, and the depositary does not guarantee. A nuanced exception is mentioned from al-Mujtaba: mixing good dirhams with counterfeit (zuyuf) dirhams does cut off the owner's right, by istihsan (juristic preference), because in practice good and bad dirhams are often mixed and cannot be practically distinguished. This demonstrates the Hanafi use of istihsan to achieve practical justice.

Key Terms
خلط (khalta): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property.
لا يتميز (la yatamayyaz): The deposit cannot be distinguished from the mixed-with property except with difficulty.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of legal reasoning that departs from strict analogy for practical considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a depositary mixes the owner's deposit of walnuts with his own almonds, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The depositary becomes a guarantor because mixing occurred without permission.
B) The depositary does not guarantee because walnuts and almonds are easily separable.
C) The depositary becomes a partner with the owner.
D) The depositary is liable only if the almonds are of greater value.

Key Takeaway
A depositary who mixes a deposit without permission only guarantees it if the mixture becomes indistinguishable with difficulty; easy separation preserves the owner's right.

Ten Exceptions to Liability When a Depositary Dies Without Specifying (Mujhilan)
Lists exceptional cases of no liability for a depositary dying mujhilan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the six examples of exceptional cases where a depositary dies without specifying the deposit (mujhilan) and explain why liability is waived in each case.

Core Idea
A depositary who dies without specifying the deposit faces no liability if the deposit is lost after death.

In Hanafi law, a depositary (wadi') is generally liable for loss or damage to a deposit if he deals with it improperly. However, a special set of exceptions exists: if the depositary dies without having informed anyone about the deposit (mujhilan), and the deposit is subsequently lost through no fault of his, the liability is lifted. This lesson examines six specific cases mentioned in Al-Ashbah, forming part of a larger list of ten (later expanded to nineteen by Al-Shurunbulali). Understanding these exceptions clarifies the boundaries of the depositary's responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule of liability for deposits
Recall that a depositary (wadi') is liable if he misuses the deposit or neglects it. However, if he dies without specifying the deposit, the loss after death is beyond his control.
The key term here is 'mujhilan' – dying without specifying or making known the deposit.
2
Examine the six specific cases listed in the passage
Read each of the six cases: executor, father, heir, person whose house receives windblown property, person whose house has property placed without knowledge, and a minor under interdiction. Note the common element: each depositary dies mujhilan.
Each case involves a special relationship or circumstance that justifies the exception.
3
Understand the underlying principle for no liability
The exception is based on the depositary having no opportunity to inform others (heirs or judge) before death, and the loss occurring after death without his fault. The depositary is not guilty of negligence or betrayal.
This is a concession (rukhsah) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Recognize that the list is not exhaustive
The passage states the author of Al-Ashbah listed ten cases, but only six are mentioned here. Later scholars like Al-Shurunbulali expanded it to nineteen. This indicates the principle is open to analogical extension.
The expansion shows the fiqh process of tatbiq (application) and takhrij (derivation).

Source Text

وفي وصي إذا مات مُجْهِلا فلا ضمان عليه، وفي أب إذا مات مجهلاً مال ابنه ، وفي وراث إذا مات مُجهلاً ما أودع عند مورثه، وفي رجل ماتَ مُجْهِلا لما ألقته الريح في بيته، وفي رجل مات مُجهلاً لما وضعه مالكه في بيته بغير علمه، وفي صبي مات مُجهلاً لما أودع . عنده محجوراً، فهذه عشرة مسائل استثناها صاحب «الأشباه»، وغيره. وأما الشرنبلالي فعد المستثناة تسعة عشر مسئلة. ذكـرهـا صـاحب «الـدر المختار» (1) وغيره نقلاً عن «شرح الوهبانية» للشرنبلالي .
And regarding an executor: if he dies having not specified [the deposit], there is no liability upon him. And regarding a father: if he dies having not specified his son's property. And regarding an heir: if he dies having not specified what was deposited with his testator. And regarding a man: if he dies having not specified what the wind threw into his house. And regarding a man: if he dies having not specified what its owner placed in his house without his knowledge. And regarding a minor: if he dies having not specified what was deposited with him while he was under interdiction. Commentary
This passage lists ten specific cases where despite the general rule that a depositary is liable if he deals with the deposit improperly, there is no liability. The common factor is that the person died without leaving clear instructions about the deposit (mujhilan), and the loss occurred after death. The ten cases are: (1) an executor (wasi) who dies without specifying, (2) a father who dies without specifying his son's property, (3) an heir (warith) who dies without specifying what was deposited with his testator, (4) a man who dies without specifying what the wind blew into his house, (5) a man who dies without specifying what an owner placed in his house without his knowledge, (6) a minor (sabi) who dies without specifying what was deposited with him while he was under interdiction (mahjur). The text says 'ten issues' but only lists six; the remaining four are presumably mentioned in the sources referenced. The author notes that al-Shurunbulali expanded the list to nineteen. This exception is based on the idea that the depositary had no intention of wrongdoing and the deposit was lost due to forces beyond his control. The ruling is from the Hanafi school and is considered a concession (rukhsah).

Key Terms
مُجْهِلا (mujhilan): Dying without having specified or made known the deposit or its details.
مَحْجُوراً (mahjuran): Under legal interdiction, i.e., a minor who is not legally competent to manage property.

Mixing Deposit with Own Property: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explains ruling and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mixing deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on mixing a deposit with the depositary's own property of the same genus and explain the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) between them.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit with same genus: Abu Hanifa rules complete destruction and liability; Sahibayn see potential partnership through division.

When a depositary mixes the deposit with his own property, the owner's right seems lost, but is it? The Hanafi school has a famous internal dispute: Abu Hanifa views mixing as complete destruction, making the depositary liable for the value, while his two disciples see it as creating a partnership if division is possible. Understanding this dispute clarifies how ownership and liability interact in cases of commingled property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the consensus cases
Note that mixing with permission creates partnership; mixing with a different genus always leads to liability by consensus. The dispute is only about mixing with the same genus without permission.
Use this baseline to isolate the contested area.
2
Understand the core dispute for same-genus mixing
The disagreement is whether automatic termination of the owner's right and liability follow, or whether the depositary has a choice to become a partner if division is possible.
The key concept is 'istihlak' (destruction) vs. 'shirkah' (partnership).
3
Compare the arguments of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Abu Hanifa argues mixing is istihlak from every aspect; the Sahibayn say it is partial destruction because the owner can access his right through division (qisma).
Notice the logical rebuttal: division cannot be a cause of partnership because it is an effect.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the depositary must compensate and becomes owner of the whole mixture. Under the Sahibayn's view, he may either compensate or become a partner.
This practical difference affects the rights of both parties.

[1] His saying: 'or mixes...etc.' means the depositary mixes the deposit with his own property without the owner's permission; because if he mixed with permission, he would be a partner in it. [The author] spoke in absolute terms about mixing, so it includes mixing like with like, or with something different like mixing oil with sesame oil, wheat with barley and with wheat, and silver with silver after melting. [The text] restricted it to the depositary being the one who mixes, because if the mixer was a stranger or someone in his household, the depositary would not be liable; liability is on the mixer, whether young or old, and his father does not guarantee. Thus it is mentioned in Al-Munyah quoting Al-Khulasa. [2] His saying: 'until it becomes indistinguishable...etc.' means such that the mixed item cannot be distinguished from that which it is mixed with. This is if the phrase is in the masculine form as in most copies. But if it is in the feminine form as in some copies, then the meaning is such that that deposited item which is mixed cannot be distinguished from that with which it was mixed. What is meant is that it cannot be distinguished except with difficulty, not absolute impossibility of distinction. So if access to it is easy, as when mixing walnuts with almonds, black dirhams with white dirhams, then the owner's right does not terminate by consensus. As stated in Al-Munyah and others. And it is said in Al-Mujtaba Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: mixing good dirhams with counterfeit dirhams terminates the owner's right by istihsan; because good ones are not free from counterfeit, and counterfeit ones are not free from good ones; and if he poured... [Page 414] [1] Zubdat al-Nihayah li 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: If he mixed with a different genus, the owner's right terminates and liability is obligatory by agreement. Similarly if he mixed with its genus according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly according to Abu Yusuf, except if he mixed it with something more than it; then he makes the lesser follow the greater, not with something less than it; for then the owner's right does not terminate, rather partnership is established. According to Muhammad, the owner's right does not terminate, rather partnership is established whether it is less or more. If inferior quality is mixed with good quality, he guarantees the like of the good because it becomes defective, and in the opposite he is a partner because the inferior does not become defective by the good. End. [1] His saying: 'For he...etc.' means: If the depositary mixes the deposit with a different genus, as when mixing wheat with barley, barley with wheat, oil with sesame oil, sesame oil with oil, the owner's right terminates and liability is obligatory on the depositary because this is real destruction, so it necessitates liability by consensus. And if he mixes it with its genus, as when mixing wheat with wheat in a non-liquid, milk with milk in a liquid, the depositary is liable because he becomes a destroyer of it. And if he becomes liable, he owns it and the owner's right from that deposit terminates in liquids and other things. This is according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa). And the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he mixes it with its genus, he has a choice: he can either be liable or have partnership, because he cannot access his right in form (physically), but he can access it in meaning through division; because division in things measured by weight or volume is separation, and it becomes defined by consensus; therefore each of the two partners has the right to take his own share without consent or court order. And since mixing is a destruction in one aspect (because he cannot reach the exact physical object) but not in another aspect (because he can access it in meaning), he may lean towards the side of destruction and guarantee its like, or he may lean towards the side of continuity and become a partner in the mixture. And for Abu Hanifa: mixing is destruction from every aspect; because destruction is nothing but this, i.e., making it defective, and the annihilation of the substance is by Allah's creation; therefore he has the right to claim liability by consensus, and that is a sign of destruction. And the response to their statement: that division is not considered because it is a consequence of partnership; each gets his right, so it cannot be a cause for partnership; because the effect of a cause cannot be the cause of the cause.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling of mixing a deposit (wadi'ah) with the depositary's own property without the owner's permission. It begins by clarifying that if the owner permits the mixing, then it becomes a partnership (shirkah). The commentary then discusses two key conditions: (1) the mixer is the depositary himself; if a stranger mixes, the depositary is not liable, but the stranger is. (2) the mixing must be such that the deposit becomes indistinguishable from the mixer's property, but only with difficulty (not absolute impossibility). If it remains easily distinguishable, the owner's right does not terminate by consensus. The passage then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: According to Abu Hanifa, mixing with the same genus terminates the owner's right and the depositary becomes liable (and thus becomes owner of the whole mixture). According to Abu Yusuf, if the depositary mixes the deposit with something of the same genus but in a greater quantity, the lesser follows the greater, so partnership is established; if with an equal or lesser quantity, liability applies. According to Muhammad, mixing with the same genus always results in partnership, regardless of quantity. The passage also discusses mixing with different genus (always liability by consensus), and mixing good with bad quality. The commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view that mixing is complete destruction (istihlak) because the owner's property is no longer identifiable, and the ability to divide is not sufficient to establish partnership, as division is a consequence of partnership, not a cause. The Hanafi response to the Sahibayn is that division cannot be used to justify partnership because it would make the effect of a cause serve as the cause itself, which is circular. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is generally that of Abu Hanifa on this issue, as it is considered stronger in reasoning.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing the deposit with the depositary's own property without permission, such that they become indistinguishable.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Complete consumption or destruction of the deposit such that the owner's right to the specific item is lost.
قسمة (qisma): Division of jointly owned property among partners; in this context, division of mixed goods of the same genus that are measurable (weight or volume).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The depositary becomes a partner.
B) The depositary must compensate and the owner's right terminates.
C) The owner can demand his share in kind.
D) The contract becomes void.

Key Takeaway
Al-Mukhtalaf Fih: The mixing of a deposit with the depositary's own property of the same genus is considered complete destruction (istihlak) by Abu Hanifa, leading to liability, while the Sahibayn see it as partial destruction allowing partnership; the mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Mixing Deposits with Same Genus
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing deposits with same genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three Hanafi positions on mixing a deposit with its own genus and evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
When a deposit is mixed with its own kind, Abu Hanifa sees full consumption; the Sahibayn see optional partnership.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on a crucial issue of amanah: what happens when a depositary mixes a deposit with an item of the same genus? The ruling differs sharply among the three great Imams, and the tahlil (causal analysis) reveals deep principles about legal consumption (istihlak) and shared ownership (shirkah). By studying this ikhtilaf, you will learn not only the substantive fiqh but also how Hanafi proofs are constructed and rebutted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Unanimous Rule
Start with the part of the passage that states the rule for mixing with a different genus. This is agreed upon by all Hanafis and provides a baseline for the dispute.
The rule: mixing with a different genus cuts off the owner's right and obligates guarantee (daman).
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Proof
Find where the text gives the Imam's view: mixing with the same genus is exactly like mixing with a different genus – full consumption. Note his argument that consumption is nothing but causing defect (al-ta'yib), and the physical disappearance of the substance is irrelevant.
Key phrase: 'al-khalṭ istihlāk min kulli wajh'.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Proof
Identify the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on quantity; Muhammad always holds partnership. Their common proof: division (qismah) is possible, so the owner can access his right legally, thus mixing is only partial consumption.
Abu Yusuf: if deposit is less, it's consumption; if more, partnership. Muhammad: always partnership.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Rebuttal
Read Abu Hanifa's response to the Sahibayn: division is a consequence of partnership, not a cause. The effect cannot be the cause of the cause. This is a classic Hanafi usul argument about causality.
The rebuttal: 'ḥukm al-illah lā yakūnu 'illatan lil-'illah'.
5
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Find the practical difference illustrated at the end: if the owner discharges (ibrā') the depositary, Abu Hanifa says the owner has no claim (right becomes a debt and falls), while the Sahibayn say the option of guarantee falls but partnership remains.
This shows how the ikhtilaf affects real legal outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه إن خَلَطَ بخلافِ الجنس ينقطع حقُّ المالكِ ويجب الضمان اتفاقاً، وكذا إن خلطه بجنسه عند أبي حنيفة ، وكذا عند أبي يوسف إلا إذا خَلَطَهُ بما هو أكثر منه ، يجعل الأقل تابعاً للأكثر لا بما هو أقل منه ، فإنَّه لا ينقطع حق المالك ، بل يُثبت الشركة، وعند محمد الله لا ينقطع حق المالك، بل يثبت الشركة سواء كان أقل أو أكثر. الرديء على الجيد يضمن مثل الجيد ؛ لأنه تعيب، وفي عكسه كان شريكاً ؛ لأن الرديء لا يتعيب بالجيد. انتهى(۱). [١] قوله : فإنه ... الخ ؛ يعني فإنَّ المودَعَ إن خلط الوديعة بغير جنسها كما إذا خلط البر بالشعير، والشعير بالبرّ، والزيت بالشيرج، والشيرج بالزيت ينقطع حق المالك ، ويجب الضمان على المودع ؛ لأنَّ هذا استهلاك حقيقية، فيوجب الضمان بالإجماع، وإن خلطها بجنسها. كما إذا خلط البرِّ بالبرّ في غير المائع، واللبن باللبن في المائع ضَمِنَ المودع ؛ لأنه صار مستهلكاً لها ، وإذا ضمنها ملكها وانقطع حقُّ المالك من تلك الوديعة في المائع وغيره، وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : إذا خلطها بجنسها شركةً إن شاء ؛ لأنه لا يمكنه الوصول إلى حقه صورة، وأمكنه معنى بالقسمة ؛ لأنَّ القسمة فيما يكال أو يوزن إفراز، ويتعين بالإجماع ؛ ولذا يملك كل واحد من الشريكين أن يأخذ حصة نفسه بلا رضاء وقضاء، وإذا كان الخلط استهلاكاً من وجه حيث لا يمكنه الوصول إلى عين حقه صورة دون وجه حيث أمكنه معنى، فإن شاءَ مالَ إلى جانب الهلاك، وضمنه مثله ، وإن شاءَ مالَ إلى جانب القيام وشارك المخلوط. ولأبي حنيفة : إن الخلط استهلاك من كلِّ وجه؛ لأنه ليس الاستهلاك إلا هذا، وهو التعييب، وأمّا انعدام المحل فبتخليق الله تعالى ؛ ولهذا كان له حق التضمين بالإجماع، وهو إمارة الاستهلاك. والجواب عن قولهما : إنّه لا اعتبار بالقسمة ؛ لأنّها توجبها الشركة، فيصل كلُّ واحد حقه، فلا تصلح أن تكون موجبة للشركة ؛ لأنَّ حكم العلة لا يكون علة العلة. ثم قالوا : لا يباح للخالط التناول قبل أداء الضمان، ولو أبرأ المودَعُ الخالط لا سبيل للمودع على المخلوط عند الإمام ؛ لأنَّ حقه لم يكن إلا في الدين، وقد سقط بإبرائه، وعندهما بالإبراء يسقط خيار الضمان ، فتكون الشركة في المخلوط ، فظهر ثمرة الخلاف.
If he mixes with a different genus, the owner's right is cut off and guarantee is obligatory unanimously. Similarly, if he mixes it with its own genus according to Abu Hanifa. Similarly according to Abu Yusuf, except if he mixes it with what is more than it: he makes the lesser a follower of the greater, not with what is less than it; then the owner's right is not cut off, rather partnership is established. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), the owner's right is not cut off, rather partnership is established whether less or more. Inferior with superior: he guarantees the like of the superior because it became defective. In its opposite, he is a partner because the inferior does not become defective by the superior. End(1). [1] His statement: 'For he...' etc.; meaning that if the depositary mixes the deposit with something other than its genus, such as mixing wheat with barley, barley with wheat, oil with sesame oil, sesame oil with oil, the owner's right is cut off and guarantee is obligatory on the depositary because this is real consumption, thus it necessitates guarantee by consensus. And if he mixes it with its own genus, as when he mixes wheat with wheat in non-liquid, and milk with milk in liquid, the depositary is liable because he has become a consumer of it. And when he is liable, he owns it, and the owner's right is cut off from that deposit in liquid and otherwise. This is according to the Imam. They (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: If he mixes it with its own genus, it is a partnership if he wishes, because he cannot reach his right in form (physically), but he can in meaning (legally) through division, because division in what is measured or weighed is separation, and it becomes definite by consensus. Hence each of the two partners can take his own share without consent or court order. And since the mixing is consumption in one sense (where he cannot reach the exact physical property) but not in another sense (where he can reach it legally), if he wishes, he leans toward the aspect of destruction and guarantees its like, and if he wishes, he leans toward the aspect of existence and becomes a partner in the mixture. Abu Hanifa's proof: Mixing is consumption in every sense, because consumption is nothing but this, and it is causing defect. As for the disappearance of the substance, it is by Allah's creation, hence he has the right to claim guarantee by consensus, and this is an indication of consumption. The response to their argument: Division is not considered because division is caused by partnership, so each person receives his right; it cannot be a cause for partnership because the effect of a cause cannot be the cause of the cause. Then they said: The mixer is not permitted to consume before paying the guarantee. And if the owner discharges the mixer, the depositor has no claim on the mixture according to the Imam because his right was only a debt, and it falls by discharge. According to them, discharge removes the option of guarantee, so partnership in the mixture remains. Thus the practical difference appears.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) on the ruling when a depositary mixes a deposit with another substance. It begins by stating the unanimous rule: mixing with a different genus (e.g., wheat with barley) cuts off the owner's right and obligates the depositary to guarantee (daman). The dispute arises when mixing with the same genus (e.g., wheat with wheat). According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this also constitutes consumption (istihlak) in every sense, so the owner's right is cut off and the depositary must guarantee, thereby owning the mixture. According to Imam Abu Yusuf, the ruling depends on quantities: if the deposit is lesser, it is treated as a follower of the greater, so partnership is not established; rather the depositary guarantees and owns the mixture. But if the deposit is greater, then the owner's right remains and partnership is established. According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, regardless of quantity, mixing with the same genus creates shared ownership (shirkah); the owner retains his right and the depositary becomes a partner. The commentary then elaborates the reasoning: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that since division (qismah) is possible (for measurable or weighable items), the owner can access his right legally through division, so mixing is not full consumption; the depositary has the option to either guarantee or accept partnership. Abu Hanifa responds that division is a result of partnership, not a cause for it; mixing is consumption because it renders the specific property indistinguishable, and the physical disappearance is irrelevant. The passage concludes with a practical difference: if the owner discharges the depositary, according to Abu Hanifa the owner has no claim (since the right became a debt), while according to the Sahibayn the option of guarantee falls and partnership remains.

Key Terms
خلط (khalṭ): Mixing a deposit with another substance, whether of the same or different genus, causing indistinguishability.
وديعة (wadīʿah): A deposit placed in another's custody for safekeeping without compensation.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate the owner with an equivalent item or value due to consumption or loss.
شركة (shirkah): Shared ownership in the mixture according to each party's contribution.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Legal consumption: rendering the owner unable to access his specific property physically, leading to liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, what happens when a depositary mixes a deposit with its own kind, regardless of quantity?
A) The owner's right is cut off and the depositary must guarantee.
B) The owner has the option to either take a guarantee or become a partner.
C) The owner becomes a partner in the mixture.
D) The depositary becomes the owner and must provide an equivalent.

Key Takeaway
Mixing a deposit with its own genus triggers an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Abu Hanifa sees full consumption requiring guarantee, while the Sahibayn see optional partnership, with the thamarah al-khilaf appearing when the owner discharges the depositary.

Mixing Deposits of Different Genus: Liquids and Grains
Ruling on mixing deposits of different genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a liquid deposit is mixed with a liquid of a different genus, explain the qiyas to mixing wheat and barley, and identify the key condition (impossibility of division) that triggers consumption.

Core Idea
Mixing deposits of different genus cuts the owner's right; the mixture is treated as consumption because division is impossible.

This lesson explores the Hanafi ruling on mixing deposits of different genera. Whether it is oil with sesame oil or wheat with barley, the principle is that the owner loses his specific right to his property and is entitled to compensation. The key reasoning is that division (qismah) is impossible when the items are of different types and cannot be perfectly separated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Distinguish between mixing liquids of different genera and mixing grains of different types (wheat and barley).
2
Analyze the ruling
For liquids of different genus, the passage states unequivocally that the depositary must guarantee and the owner's right is cut off because it is consumption in form and meaning.
3
Examine the qiyas (analogy)
The passage analogizes mixing wheat and barley to the liquid case. Understand the illa: impossibility of perfect division leads to hardship, and difficulty is treated as impossibility.
Note the use of the maxim 'al-muta'assir ka al-muta'addir'.
4
Note the divergent views
The passage mentions that some say the owner retains an option, and some say the qiyas would make the mixture the property of the mixer, but istihsan overrides. Identify which position is relied upon.
The relied-upon position is that the owner's right is cut off with guarantee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو خلط المائع بخلاف جنسه ؛ كما إذا خلط الزيت بالشيرج، يوجب انقطاع حق المالك بالضمان ؛ لأنه استهلاكه صورة ومعنى ، لتعذر القسمة باختلاف الجنس، وتعين المبادلة من هذا القبيل . خلط الحنطة بالشعير في الصحيح؛ لأنه لا يصل المالك إلى عين ملكه إلا بحرج، والمتعسر كالمتعدّر ؛ ولأنَّ الحنطة لا تخلو عن الشعير، والشعير لا يخلو عن حبات الحنطة، فتعذر التمييز حقيقة وحكماً أيضاً بالقسمة؛ لاختلاف الجنس؛ لأنَّ القسمة عند اختلاف الجنس غير مشروع. وقيل : لا ينقطع حق المالك عن المخلوط هاهنا ، ويكون له الخيار، وقيل: القياس أن يصير المخلوط ملكاً للخالط عند الإمام، وفي الاستحسان: لا يصير. كما ذكره في «الكفاية)، وغيرها. ولو خلط المائع بجنسه فعند الإمام ينقطع حق المالكِ إلى الضمان ؛ لِمَا ذكرنا من استهلاكه من كل وجه عنده ، وعند أبي يوسف يجعل الأقل تابعاً للأكثر اعتباراً لغالب أجزاءه ، فيكون المخلوط لصاحب الكثير ، ويضمن لصاحب القليل اعتباراً. وعند محمد الله شركة مطلقاً سواء كان أحدهما غالباً أو مغلوباً ، أو كانا متساويين ؛ لأنَّ الجنس لا يغلب الجنس عنده ، كما إذا جمع بين لبن امرأتين في قدح وصب في حلق رضيع يثبت الرضاعُ منهما عند محمد الله وعند أبي يوسف العبرة للكثرة كما صرّحوا في أحكام الرضاع، فقد عرفت أنَّ في كلام الشارح له إجمالاً في محلّ التفصيل، وأوضحت المقام بكمل التوضيح، فتذكر وتشكر.
And if he mixes a liquid with a different genus, such as mixing oil with sesame oil, it necessitates cutting off the owner's right with guarantee, because it is consumption in form and meaning, due to the impossibility of division with difference of genus, and exchange is determined from this category. Mixing wheat with barley is correct [as similar], because the owner cannot reach his specific property except with hardship, and what is difficult is like impossible. And because wheat is not free of barley, and barley is not free of wheat grains, so distinction is impossible in reality and also legally by division, because division with difference of genus is not legislated. And it is said: The owner's right is not cut off regarding the mixture here, and he has the option. And it is said: The analogy (qiyas) is that the mixture becomes the property of the mixer according to the Imam, and in istihsan it does not become his. As mentioned in al-Kifayah and others. And if he mixes a liquid with its own genus, then according to the Imam the owner's right is cut off to guarantee, due to what we mentioned of its consumption in every sense according to him. According to Abu Yusuf, he makes the lesser a follower of the greater in consideration of the majority of its parts, so the mixture belongs to the owner of the majority, and he guarantees for the owner of the lesser in consideration. According to Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), it is partnership absolutely, whether one is dominant or dominated, or they are equal, because genus does not overcome genus according to him. Just as if he collects milk of two women in a cup and pours it into the throat of a nursing infant, breastfeeding is established from both according to Muhammad, while according to Abu Yusuf, consideration is given to the majority, as they stated in the rulings of breastfeeding. You have known that in the speech of the commentator there is brevity in a place requiring detail, and I have clarified the matter with complete clarity, so remember and be grateful.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on mixing deposits, focusing specifically on liquids and grains. It first addresses mixing a liquid deposit with a different genus (e.g., oil with sesame oil). This is unequivocally considered consumption in both form and meaning because division (qismah) is impossible due to difference in genus; therefore, the owner's right is cut off and the depositary must guarantee. The same ruling applies to mixing wheat with barley, as the grains cannot be perfectly separated without hardship, and difficulty renders it equivalent to impossibility. The author notes a minority view that the owner retains an option, but the relied-upon position (as per Imam Abu Hanifa) is that the mixture becomes the property of the mixer through guarantee, while istihsan (according to some) suggests otherwise. The passage then returns to mixing a liquid with its own genus, reiterating the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa rules consumption and guarantee, Abu Yusuf rules based on majority quantity (the lesser is follower, so the owner of the majority gets the mixture and guarantees the minority), and Muhammad al-Shaybani rules partnership regardless of quantity. An analogy from breastfeeding law is provided: if milk from two women is mixed and fed to an infant, Muhammad holds that both become foster mothers, while Abu Yusuf considers the majority. The commentator concludes by clarifying that he has elaborated the details to resolve any ambiguity in the original commentary.

Key Terms
مائع (māʾiʿ): Liquid (e.g., oil, milk, sesame oil).
قسمة (qismah): Division/partition of shared property; considered possible for measurable items but not for liquids of different genus.
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; used here to set aside qiyas (analogy) in favor of a different ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the illa (effective cause) for treating mixing wheat and barley the same as mixing oil and sesame oil?
A) Both are solid items.
B) Both are of different genera, making legal division impossible.
C) Both are food items.
D) The mixture becomes impure.

Key Takeaway
When deposits of different genus are mixed, the depositary must compensate; the owner loses the specific right to his property because division is impossible.

Mixing Deposits of Same Genus: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing same-genus liquid deposits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on mixing liquid deposits of the same genus, and explain the practical consequences of each view.

Core Idea
When mixing the same genus liquids, Hanafi imams differ: consumption, majority ownership, or partnership.

This lesson details the intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing liquid deposits of the same genus. While all agree on the context, they differ on the legal effect: Imam Abu Hanifa holds it is consumption, Abu Yusuf rules based on majority, and Muhammad al-Shaybani says partnership. The passage also draws an analogy from breastfeeding to support Muhammad's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common scenario
Mixing two deposits of the same liquid genus (e.g., milk with milk).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa: mixing cuts the owner's right requiring guarantee (consumption).
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf: the majority determines ownership; the lesser is follower and its owner gets compensation.
4
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad: partnership regardless of quantity; both owners become partners in the mixture.
5
Examine the breastfeeding analogy
Muhammad supports his view with an analogy: mixing milk from two women and feeding an infant establishes fosterage from both. This shows that mixing same genus does not override the rights of each contributor.
Abu Yusuf counters by considering the majority in breastfeeding.

The Hanafi school has three positions on mixing same-genus liquids: consumption (Abu Hanifa), majority ownership (Abu Yusuf), and partnership (Muhammad). The practical ruling depends on which opinion is deemed strongest.

Mixing Deposits of Different Types: Liability and Its Foundation
Liability for mixing deposits of different types, with analogical extension to grains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a depositary mixes a deposited item with a different type and explain the reasoning (ta'lil) based on the impossibility of division.

Core Idea
Mixing a deposit with a different type cuts off the owner's right and makes the depositary liable because division between different types is impossible.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling for a common scenario: a depositary mixes the deposited item, say olive oil, with a different type like sesame oil. The key question is whether the owner can still claim his specific item. The passage explains that mixing with a different type amounts to consumption (istihlak) because division (qisma) is not permitted between different types, thus the depositary becomes liable. The text then extends the same reasoning to mixing wheat with barley, and also reports alternative opinions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
Determine what the depositary did (mixing a deposit with a different type) and the legal consequence (liability).
The passage begins with 'ولو خلط المائع بخلاف جنسه' – this sets the scope.
2
Understand the effective cause ('illa)
Why does mixing with a different type trigger liability? The passage cites 'تعذر القسمة' (impossibility of division) and 'حرج' (difficulty of separation).
The 'illa is that the owner cannot recover the specific item without extreme difficulty.
3
Apply the analogy (qiyas) to grains
The author extends the ruling from liquids to mixing wheat and barley, using the same 'illa of impossibility of division.
The text says 'خلط الحنطة بالشعير في الصحيح' – it is considered the correct view by analogy.
4
Note the alternative opinions
The passage reports two other views: one giving the owner an option, and another based on strict qiyas that the mixture becomes the mixer's property (but istihsan overrides).
These are introduced with 'قيل' – they are weak or overridden.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The phrase 'في الصحيح' points to the relied-upon view: the owner's right is terminated and the depositary is liable.
This is the position for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو خلط المائع بخلاف جنسه؛ كما إذا خلط الزيت بالشيرج، يوجب انقطاع حق المالك بالضمان؛ لأنه استهلاكه صورة ومعنى، لتعذر القسمة باختلاف الجنس، وتعين المبادلة من هذا القبيل. خلط الحنطة بالشعير في الصحيح؛ لأنه لا يصل المالك إلى عين ملكه إلا بحرج، والمتعسر كالمتعدّر؛ ولأنَّ الحنطة لا تخلو عن الشعير، والشعير لا يخلو عن حبات الحنطة، فتعذر التمييز حقيقة وحكماً أيضاً بالقسمة؛ لاختلاف الجنس؛ لأنَّ القسمة عند اختلاف الجنس غير مشروع. وقيل: لا ينقطع حق المالك عن المخلوط هاهنا، ويكون له الخيار، وقيل: القياس أن يصير المخلوط ملكاً للخالط عند الإمام، وفي الاستحسان: لا يصير. كما ذكره في «الكفاية)، وغيرها.
And if one mixes a liquid with a different type, such as mixing olive oil with sesame oil, it necessitates the termination of the owner's right and the [mixer's] liability, because he has consumed it in form and meaning, due to the impossibility of division because of the difference in type, and exchange becomes determined from this category. Mixing wheat with barley is [also] according to the correct opinion; because the owner cannot reach the exact substance of his property except with difficulty, and the difficult is like the impossible; and because the wheat cannot be free of barley, and the barley cannot be free of wheat grains, so separation is impossible in reality and also in ruling through division due to the difference of type, because division when types differ is not legislated. And it is said: The owner's right does not terminate from the mixture here, and he has the option. And it is said: According to analogy (qiyas), the mixture becomes the property of the mixer according to Imam Abu Hanifa, and according to istihsan, it does not become. As mentioned in al-Kifayah and other works.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when a depositary (أمين) mixes a deposited item with a different type of substance. The default ruling is that the depositary becomes liable for the value (ضمان) because by mixing with a different type, the deposited item is effectively consumed (استهلاك) both in form and meaning. The key reason is that division (قسمة) between two different types is not legally permissible, so the owner cannot recover his specific item. Thus, the depositary must compensate the owner for the value. The text then extends this ruling by analogy to the case of mixing wheat with barley, which is also considered a consumption because of the difficulty of separation in reality and legally through division. There are two alternative opinions (قيل) reported: one that the owner retains an option, and another that by strict analogy the mixture becomes the property of the mixer according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but istihsan (juristic preference) dictates otherwise. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the first one: the owner's right is terminated, and the depositary is liable.

Key Terms
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption of the deposited item, meaning the owner can no longer benefit from the specific item
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate the owner for the value of the destroyed item
قسمة (qisma): Division of the mixed substance to separate the owner's portion
جنس (jins): Type or category of item, e.g., oil vs oil, or oil vs sesame oil

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does the owner's right to the specific deposited item terminate in the case of mixing?
A) Only if the depositary admits mixing
B) When the deposited item is mixed with a different type
C) When the deposited item is mixed with the same type and the mixture is equal
D) Never, the owner always retains the right

Key Takeaway
Mixing a deposit with a different type terminates the owner's right and imposes liability on the depositary because division between different types is impossible.

Mixing Deposited Liquid with Same Type: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mixing same-type deposit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi views on mixing same-type liquids and apply them to determine ownership and liability in deposit cases.

Core Idea
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: mixing same-type liquids yields three distinct rulings on ownership.

This passage presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a depositary who mixes the deposited liquid with his own liquid of the same type. The three principal imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—each adopt a different legal consequence. Understanding their reasoning and practical implications is essential for correctly advising on deposit contracts and liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Recognize that the case involves a depositary mixing a deposited liquid with his own liquid of the same type, e.g., oil with oil, milk with milk.
The mixing must be with the same type (jins).
2
State the three views
Recall each imam's ruling: Abu Hanifa cuts off the owner's right entirely; Abu Yusuf holds that the greater quantity prevails; Muhammad holds that partnership arises regardless of quantity.
All three views are from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Analyse the proof for each view
Examine the rationale: Abu Hanifa sees the deposit as consumed; Abu Yusuf uses the principle of majority (i'tibar al-kathrah); Muhammad argues that same type does not dominate, supported by the analogy of mixed milk for breastfeeding (rida').
The rida' analogy is specific to Muhammad's argument.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Compare the practical consequences: under Abu Yusuf, the mixture belongs to the one with larger quantity; under Muhammad, both become partners proportionally.
The difference affects who owns the mixture and what compensation is due.
5
Note the absence of tarjih in this passage
The text does not pick a preferred view; later scholars often adopt Abu Yusuf's view for commercial clarity, but all three are valid positions.
A mufti must choose based on context and need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو خلط المائع بجنسه فعند الإمام ينقطع حق المالكِ إلى الضمان؛ لِمَا ذكرنا من استهلاكه من كل وجه عنده، وأبي يوسف له يجعل الأقل تابعاً للأكثر اعتباراً وعند غالب أجزاءه، فيكون المخلوط لصاحب الكثير، ويضمن لصاحب القليل اعتباراً. وعند محمد الله شركة مطلقاً سواء كان أحدهما غالباً أو مغلوباً، أو كانا متساويين؛ لأنَّ الجنس لا يغلب الجنس عنده، كما إذا جمع بين لبن امرأتين في قدح وصب في حلق رضيع يثبت الرضاعُ منهما عند محمد الله وعند أبي يوسف الله العبرة للكثرة كما صرّحوا في أحكام الرضاع، فقد عرفت أنَّ في كلام الشارح له إجمالاً في محلّ التفصيل، وأوضحت المقام بكمل التوضيح، (۱) فتذكر وتشكر (٢).
And if one mixes a liquid with its own type, then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the owner's right is cut off and [the mixer] is liable, due to what we mentioned of consumption from every perspective according to him. According to Abu Yusuf, he makes the lesser follow the greater in consideration, and according to the majority of its parts, so the mixture belongs to the owner of the greater quantity, and he is liable to the owner of the lesser quantity in consideration. According to Muhammad, there is partnership absolutely, whether one is greater or lesser, or they are equal, because, in his view, one type does not overcome another type, just as if one collects the milk of two women in a cup and pours it into the throat of an infant, the rida' is established from both according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, the consideration is for the greater quantity, as they have stated in the rulings of rida'. So you have known that in the speech of the commentator there is conciseness in a place that needs detail, and I have clarified the point with complete explanation, so remember and be grateful.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where the depositary mixes a deposited liquid with the same type of liquid (e.g., mixing the depositor's oil with his own oil of the same kind). The three principal imams of the Hanafi school differ. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the owner's right to the specific item is entirely terminated, and the mixer is liable for its value, because the deposited item is considered consumed from every perspective. Abu Yusuf considers the majority: the mixture becomes the property of the one who had the larger quantity, and he must compensate the owner of the smaller portion. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, however, holds that both parties become partners in the mixture in proportion to their shares, regardless of which quantity is larger; because, in his view, items of the same type do not cause one to dominate the other. He supports this with an analogy to breastfeeding: if the milk of two women is mixed and fed to an infant, the child becomes a foster child to both women, even if one's milk was more. This intra-school dispute has practical consequences for the liability and ownership of the mixture.

Key Terms
شركة (shirkah): Partnership in the mixture, i.e., joint ownership in proportion to each party's original quantity
اعتبار الكثرة (i'tibar al-kathrah): The legal principle that the larger quantity determines the ownership of the mixture

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf, if a depositary mixes a 1-liter deposit of oil with 9 liters of his own oil (same type), who owns the mixture?
A) The depositor
B) The depositary
C) Both in partnership
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, mixing a deposited liquid with the same type does not have a single ruling; the three imams differ on ownership and liability, and a mufti must choose based on context.

Liability for Misuse and Improper Storage of a Deposit
Rulings on depositary's misuse and violation of storage instructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts of ta'addi that trigger liability for a depositary and apply the Hanafi ruling on storage violations and mixing cases.

Core Idea
Ta'addi makes a depositary liable; mixing gives owner the choice to claim compensation or partner.

A deposit (wadi'ah) is a trust, but when the depositary exceeds the bounds of trust, he becomes liable. This lesson examines the key acts of ta'addi—using the deposit, spending and mixing, and storing in a less secure location—that trigger liability. We will also explore the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether spending part and mixing constitutes complete consumption, and how the owner's option resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts of ta'addi
Read the passage and list the specific actions that constitute ta'addi: wearing a cloth, riding an animal, using a slave, spending part and mixing, storing in a different house.
The passage also mentions detention after demand and denial.
2
Determine the liability condition for storage
Note that liability for storage in the wrong place only arises if the alternative location is less secure; if equally secure, the depositary is not liable.
This is a key nuance: the condition is enforceable only when it serves a protective purpose.
3
Analyze the mixing scenario
Understand that when the depositary spends part and then mixes its like with the remainder, the owner has an option according to the Sahibayn: either hold the depositary liable for the whole or become a partner in the mixture. Abu Hanifa considers it complete consumption, denying the partnership option.
The mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn's view.
4
Apply the rules to a scenario
Given a set of facts (e.g., depositary drives the car without permission, or stores money in a different but equally secure safe), determine whether liability arises.
Focus on the conditions: use without permission always triggers liability; storage violation only if less secure; spending+mixing gives owner choice.

Commentary
This passage enumerates several acts of transgression (تعدي) by the depositary that trigger liability (ضمان). These include: using the deposit (wearing a cloth, riding an animal, employing a slave), spending part of the deposit and then replenishing it with its like and mixing it with the rest, and storing the deposit in a location different from what the owner specified. The commentary clarifies that for the storage violation, the depositary is liable only if the alternative location is less secure. If the rooms are equally secure, the condition is not considered enforceable because it would be impossible to observe. Additionally, the passage notes that if the depositary detains the deposit after the owner's demand or denies its existence, the deposit contract ends and he becomes a usurper (غاصب), leading to liability. For the case of spending part and mixing, the owner has an option: either hold the depositary liable for the value or become a partner in the mixture. The passage ends with a reference to Abu Hanifa's view that consumption is complete.

Key Terms
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression by using the deposit without permission or acting against the owner's instructions
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper, one who takes possession of another's property unlawfully

Cases of Depositary's Liability for Misuse (Ta'addi)
Explains five scenarios where depositary becomes liable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five scenarios where a depositary becomes liable for misuse, explain the condition for liability in storing in a different location, and state the intra-Hanafi difference regarding mixing after spending.

Core Idea
The depositary's unauthorized use or mishandling of the deposit triggers liability, but the owner's option varies per the Imam's view on consumption.

In this passage, the commentator elaborates on the matn's ruling that a depositary becomes liable (daman) when he exceeds the limits of the trust. We will examine each scenario, understand when a condition to store in a specific place is binding, and explore the legal reasoning behind liability, including a notable difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five acts of ta'addi
From the passage, list the actions that constitute exceeding limits: wearing the deposited garment, riding the animal, spending part and mixing with the like, and storing in a location other than the one ordered. Also note the implicit case of detaining after demand and denying the deposit.
The commentary adds that using a slave also counts.
2
Understand the condition for liability in the storage scenario
The commentator explains that if the owner orders storage in a specific room and the depositary stores in another room of the same house, liability only arises if the other room is less secure. If both rooms are equally safe, the condition is ineffective and no liability.
This shows that a condition must be beneficial to be binding.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning for each case
The passage gives reasons: detention after demand ends the contract, making the depositary a usurper; denial also terminates the contract; using the item is consumption (istihlak); mixing after spending is partial consumption according to the two disciples, and full consumption according to Abu Hanifa.
Understanding the rationale helps in analogizing new cases.
4
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on mixing after spending
Imam Abu Hanifa views the act as full consumption, giving the owner the choice to claim full compensation or become a partner in the mixture. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad see it as partial consumption, giving the owner either to claim compensation or share, but not both.
The mufta bihi position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa.

معنى.
وأما في صورة الخلط ، فلما بيناه مفصلاً، وأما في صورة التعدي ؛ فلانه استهلاكه
وأما في صورة إنفاق البعض ثمّ خلط مثله بالباقي ؛ فلأنه خلط مال غيره بماله ، وهذا استهلاك من وجه عندهما، فيكون صاحب الوديعة بالخيار حينئذ إن شاءَ ضَمَّنَه ، وإن شاء شاركه في المخلوط ، أو من كل وجه عند الإمام، فلا يكون للمودع على
Or if the depositary exceeds limits by wearing her garment, or riding her animal, or spending some of it then mixing the like with what remains, or keeping (the deposit) in a house other than the one the owner ordered him to keep it in, he is liable. (Explanation): meaning he kept it in a house other than the one he was ordered to keep it in. His statement 'he is liable' is the result of the condition, which is his statement: 'if he detains it...' [1] His statement: 'or exceeds...' etc.; meaning the depositary exceeded limits in the deposit, such as if it was a garment and he wore it, or an animal and he rode it, and likewise a slave and he used him, or he spent some of the deposit then returned the like and mixed it with the remainder of the deposit, or he kept that deposit in a house regarding which the owner of the deposit ordered him to keep it in other than that house. [2] His statement: 'or keeps in a house' etc.; it is restricted by his saying 'in a house'; because if the owner ordered the depositary to keep the deposit in a specific room within the depositary's house, and the depositary kept it in another room of that house, and the rooms were equal in security, the depositary is not liable; because he cannot observe safe-keeping while observing this condition, so it is not beneficial. So the condition is only considered if in that other room there is a apparent deficiency, such as the house containing the two rooms being large, and the room he prohibited him from keeping in being exposed and feared for it; then the condition is considered, and the depositary is liable because the specified one is more secure than the other, as will come to you if Allah wills. [3] His statement: 'he is liable' in all these cases. As for if he detained it after its owner demanded it, due to the transgression of his prevention; this is because when he demanded it, he was not pleased with his retention after the demand, so he is liable for his detention of it. Similarly if he denied it (the deposit) in the presence of its owner; because by the demand the contract of deposit ends, and he becomes a usurper (ghasib) by his retention after it. As for the case of mixing, as we detailed earlier. As for the case of transgression, because it is consumption (istihlak). As for the case of spending some then mixing the like with the remainder, because he mixed another's property with his own, and this is consumption from one aspect according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), so the owner of the deposit has the option then: if he wishes he makes him liable, and if he wishes he shares with him in the mixture, or from every aspect according to the Imam (Abu Hanifa), so the depositary has no (option?)...
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn listing cases where the depositary (mudi') becomes liable for loss. The commentator explains each scenario: (1) wearing a garment, (2) riding an animal, (3) spending part of the deposit and replacing it with a similar amount then mixing, (4) keeping the deposit in a house other than the one ordered. For the fourth scenario, a crucial condition is added: if the alternative location is equally safe, the condition is ineffective and no liability arises; but if it is clearly less secure, the depositary is liable. The commentator then explains the reasoning for liability in each case: for detention after demand, the contract ends and retention becomes usurpation; for denial, it terminates the contract; for use, it is consumption; for mixing after spending, there is a difference between Abu Hanifa (who considers it complete consumption giving the owner full option) and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) (who consider it partial consumption, giving the owner option to either claim compensation or become a partner in the mixture). This intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf affects the owner's recourse.

Key Terms
تعدي (ta'addi): Exceeding the limits of the deposit contract; any unauthorized use or handling of the deposited property.
ضمان (daman): Liability to compensate for loss or damage; here, the depositary becomes responsible for the value if the item is lost due to his transgression.
خلط (khalat): Mixing the deposited property with the depositary's own property, which can create joint ownership if done without permission or after transgression.
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption; using the property in a way that it cannot be returned in kind.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where the depositary spends part of the deposit and then replaces it with similar and mixes it with the remainder, what option does the owner have according to Imam Abu Hanifa?
A) Only to claim compensation
B) Only to become a partner in the mixture
C) Either to claim compensation or to become a partner in the mixture
D) No option; the mixture is owned by the depositary

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the depositary's liability for misuse arises from the act of transgression itself, not merely from loss, and the owner's options vary based on the nature of the misuse.

Liability After Transgression: Does Returning the Deposit Remove Liability?
Ruling on liability when mixing occurs without action and whether removing transgression removes liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that removing transgression removes liability and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a deposit contract continues despite transgression; thus, stopping the transgression ends liability.

The depositary who unlawfully uses a deposit becomes liable for its loss. But what if he stops using and returns it to proper care? The Hanafi school holds that removing the transgression restores the original trust, while the Shafi'i school argues the contract is terminated and liability persists. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind this key difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage states: (a) mixing without action leads to partnership; (b) removing transgression ceases liability.
First ruling is by consensus; second is disputed.
2
Understand the Shafi'i objection
Shafi'i (and Malik, Ahmad in one narration) argue that transgression terminates the deposit contract; once terminated, it cannot be revived without a new contract. Hence, guarantee persists.
Their evidence: the item became guaranteed (madhmun) due to transgression.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafi scholars respond that the deposit contract is absolute, covering all times before and after transgression. Use is not meant to invalidate the contract nor does it contradict the essence of deposit. Therefore, the contract remains valid, and removing transgression restores the original state.
Key: the contract is not 'void' by transgression; only the depositary's trustworthiness is temporarily compromised.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Depositary rides the deposited animal, then returns it to the stable. Under Hanafi, if the animal later dies naturally, no liability. Under Shafi'i, he remains liable because the contract was terminated.
This is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).
5
Note the condition for liability removal
If the use caused damage to the item (e.g., the animal is injured during riding), the depositary is liable for that damage even after removing transgression. Liability only ceases for future losses not caused by the transgression.
Cessation is beneficial only if no diminution occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(۱) الوديعة سبيل كما حققناه، وأما في صورة حفظ الوديعة في دار أمر به في غيرها ، فلتفاوت الدارين في الأغلب، فيكون أمر صاحب الوديعة مفيداً.

For example, if he placed it in another house, then returned it to the house the owner ordered to keep it in, liability ceases. [2] His statement: 'and if he removes' etc.; meaning if the depositary removes the transgression by leaving off wearing the garment or riding the animal, his liability ceases. And the cessation of liability is beneficial only if the use did not diminish it; if it diminished it, he is liable for the deficiency, because he becomes a detainer of a part of it on the basis of transgression. This is mentioned by Allamah Al-Tahtawi quoting from Sharh Tanwir al-Adhhan. [3] His statement: 'and according to Al-Shafi'i' etc.; meaning according to him, liability does not cease when transgression ceases. And Malik in one narration and Ahmad also hold this view; because the contract of deposit is terminated by the transgression, as evidenced by the item becoming subject to guarantee. And if the contract is terminated, it cannot return except by a new cause, and that does not exist. Our evidence: The deposit (contract) is absolute, encompassing all times before and after the transgression, and it is not invalidated by the transgression; because something is invalidated by what is placed to invalidate it or by what contradicts it, and use is not placed to invalidate the deposit, nor does it contradict it.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage addresses two rulings. First, if the deposited property mixes with the depositary's own property without any action on his part, they become partners in the mixture by consensus. No liability arises because there is no transgression. Second, if the depositary removes his transgression (e.g., stops using the garment or returns the item to the approved place), his liability ceases. However, this cessation is only meaningful if the item was not damaged by the use; if damaged, he remains liable for the deficiency. The commentator then presents the Shafi'i (and in one narration, Maliki and Hanbali) view that once liability arises, it cannot be removed even if the transgression is removed, because the contract of deposit is terminated by the transgression. The Hanafi response is that the deposit contract is ongoing and not terminated by mere use, because use does not contradict the purpose of deposit; the contract is absolute and covers all times before and after the transgression. This disagreement affects whether the depositary can return the item without compensation after a transgression.

Key Terms
اختلاط (ikhtilat): Mixing of deposited property with the depositary's own property without intentional action.
إزالة التعدي (izalat al-ta'addi): Removing the transgression by ceasing the unauthorized use and returning the item to the approved state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if the depositary rides the deposited animal (transgression), then returns it to the stable (removes transgression), and the animal later dies naturally, is the depositary liable?
A) Yes, because he once transgressed
B) No, because he removed the transgression and the animal died without fault
C) Yes, because the contract ended
D) Only if the death was caused by the ride

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the depositary who ceases his transgression before loss occurs is no longer liable for subsequent loss, because the deposit contract remains valid and liability depends on ongoing transgression.

Liability in Deposits: Does Removing Trespass End Liability?
Inter-madhhab dispute on removal of liability after trespass

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position that removing trespass (ta'addi) eliminates liability (daman) in a deposit (wadi'ah) and contrast it with the Shafi'i view, identifying the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Removing trespass restores the original non-liability – the deposit contract remains intact.

In this passage, the author discusses an inter-madhhab dispute about whether a depositary who used a deposited item without permission can later be freed from liability by stopping the use. The Hanafi school treats the trespass as a temporary suspension of the contract's ruling, not its cancellation, while the Shafi'i school views the trespass as permanently voiding the contract.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling in the matn: if the depositary removes the trespass (e.g., stops wearing the garment), his liability ceases for any future loss.
This is based on the phrase 'wa law azala al-ta'di zala damanuhu'.
2
Identify the opposing view
The commentary mentions that Imam al-Shafi'i (and Malik in one narration, Ahmad) hold that liability does not cease even after removing trespass.
Their evidence: the deposit contract is voided by the violation.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi response argues that the deposit contract is 'mutlaq' (absolute) and not voided by trespass; the use does not nullify the contract nor contradict it.
The contract's ruling (non-liability) returns when the cause of liability (trespass) is removed.
4
Examine the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument: once the deposited item becomes a guarantee (daman), the original contract is voided and cannot be revived without a new contract.
They see the guarantee status as incompatible with the trust status of a deposit.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
Work through the practical difference: a depositary wears a garment and then returns it; if it later perishes, Hanafi says no liability, Shafi'i says liability remains.
This shows the real-world impact of the legal disagreement.

We only say this because the cessation of liability in reality is not possible; because the reality of cessation of liability is after destruction, and after destruction removal of trespass is not possible. [1] His saying: 'And if it gets mixed...' etc. means: if the deposit mixes with the depositary's property without his action, then the owner of the deposit and the depositary are partners by consensus; because liability is not obligatory except due to trespass, and it (trespass) did not occur, and it was that partnership, so what perishes is from both their properties, so he is not liable. [2] His saying: 'And if he removes...' etc. means: if the depositary removes the trespass by leaving the wearing of the deposit garment, or riding its animal, his liability ceases. He only said 'his liability ceases' because liability became obligatory upon him by the very act of wearing or riding, for example, such that if it perishes during use, he is liable without dispute. [3] His saying: 'And according to al-Shafi'i...' etc. means: according to him, liability does not cease when trespass ceases. Malik in one narration and Ahmad also said that; because the contract of deposit has been nullified by the violation, as indicated by the fact that the object becomes guaranteed, and if the contract is nullified, it does not return except with a new cause, which does not exist. Our proof: The deposit is absolute, encompassing all times before and after the violation, and it is not invalidated by the violation; because the nullification of something is by that which is appointed for its nullification or by that which contradicts it, and use is not appointed to nullify the deposit, nor does it contradict it. (1) See: 'al-Nukat fi al-Masa'il al-Mukhtalaf Fiha' by al-Shirazi (p. 585). (2) In his hashya (3:380). And the answer to what al-Shafi'i used as evidence: The elevation of the ruling of the contract, which is the absence of liability, is necessary due to the establishment of its opposite, which is obligatory liability; because the ruling of the deposit contract is preservation and absence of liability, and the opposite of this contract is obligatory liability. So when its opposite is elevated, the ruling of the contract returns, as when he hires him to preserve his goods for a month, then he leaves preservation, then preserves for the remainder, so the return to the owner's deputy, i.e., the depositary, is achieved).
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the effect of removing trespass (ta'addi) on liability (daman) in a deposit (wadi'ah). The Hanafi position, as stated in the matn, is that if the depositary removes the trespass before any loss occurs, his liability ceases. For example, if he wore a deposited garment (an act of trespass) and then removed it, he is no longer liable for any subsequent loss, provided the garment was not diminished by the use. The commentary clarifies that liability attaches at the moment of trespass (e.g., wearing or riding), but if the trespass is ended, liability for future losses is removed. The Shafi'i school, along with Maliki and Hanbali in some narrations, holds that once a contract of deposit is violated by trespass, the contract is voided and the item becomes a guarantee (madhmun) in the depositary's hand. Therefore, removing trespass cannot revive the original contract; liability persists until the item is returned to the owner. The Hanafi response argues that the deposit contract is 'mutlaq' (absolute), meaning it applies to all times before and after the violation. The violation does not nullify the contract because nullification requires either a specific cause for it or something contradictory to the contract. Use (istihlak) is not specified for nullification nor does it contradict the deposit's purpose. A powerful analogy is given: if someone hires a guard for a month, the guard abandons his duty, then resumes it, his liability for losses during the neglect period remains, but for the period he resumed, it returns to the original contract. Similarly, the depositary's liability returns to non-liability once he resumes proper custody.

Key Terms
التعدي (al-ta'dī): Using the deposited property in a way not permitted by the owner, e.g., wearing a garment or riding an animal.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability to compensate for loss or damage of the deposit due to the depositary's fault (ta'di or negligence).
الوديعة (al-wadīʿah): A contract where an owner entrusts his property to another for safekeeping gratuitously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, if a depositary wears a deposited garment (an act of trespass) and then removes it and returns it to the designated spot, what happens to his liability if the garment later perishes?
A) He remains fully liable because trespass permanently voids the contract.
B) His liability ceases because he removed the trespass.
C) He is only liable if the use caused the garment to decrease in value.
D) He is not liable because the deposit contract is automatically renewed.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, liability from trespass in a deposit ceases when the trespass ends, preserving the original contract's non-liability ruling.
